"THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated (which is published price with
postage added), Books and Periodicals in the subjoined lists will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
Some Recent Publications.
Hoole’s “Science and Art of Training”
(Is. 3d.) is an admirable little handbook for
athletes and would-be athletes.
“ All Men Are Liars,” by Joseph Hocking
(3s., 4s.), is a collection of dull ideas and
descriptions likely to be popular in any semi-
educated “ religious ” household.
Lessing’s “ Laocoon ” and a selection from
his other prose writings appear in the excel-
lent Scott Library (Is. 9d.).
The new volume in the “Contemporary
Science Series” is “The Growth of the
Brain, a study of the nervous system in rela-
tion to education,” by H. H. Donaldson,
professor of neurology at Chicago University.
The book has considerable novelty, interest,
and value ; but n®t for the average reader.
An interesting book is H. B. George’s
“ Battles of English History ” (3s, 4s), done
after the style of Creasy, but without
Creasy’s stupid moralisings.
“ Everybody’s Book of Recitations”
is an acceptable addition to a popular series.
Campbell’s “ Higher English ” (Is 9d) is a
valuable little text-book on the model of
Meiklejohn’s, and contains many useful gram-
matical and philological facts dexterously put.
G. W. Smalley contributes to October
Scribner’s (Is 2d, 14s per annum) an inter-
esting gossip about Huxley. Stevenson’s
home life at Vailima is described by Lloyd
Osborne. Some good pictures of San Fran-
cisco’s Chinatown illustrate the continued
“ History of the Last Quarter-Century” in
the U.S.
Gordon and Gotch issue in the “Austra-
lian Cricket Record,” by J. Fitzpatrick, o:
Junee, N.S. W. (Is. 2d.), a publication which
is much needed, and should be much appre-
ciated. It gives a record of important
matches played during last season in the four
eastern provinces (including those with the
English team), with averages of front-rank
batsmen and principal clubs, and many
other items of information which hitherto
have been unobtainable in such compact
form.
Alex. Lindsay reports that Louis Becke’s
new book, “His Native Wife,” is selling
splendidly, and the large edition printed is
already nearly exhausted.
“ Foreseeing that some who do not love
me will be swift to allege that in the prepara-
tion of these memoirs I have set down only
such things as redound to my credit, and
have suppressed the many experiences
not so propitious which fall to the lot
of the most sagacious while in power,
I take this opportunity of refuting that
calumny. ’ Is it not pleasant, when wearied
with the uncouth hurry of nowadays “litera-
ture,” to meet such restful grace of style
in the first sentence of Weyman’s new book,
“ From the Memoirs of a Minister of
France” (Cassell and Co., 3s., 4s.)? One
willingly accompanies so courteous an author,
even when the interest of his ciceronish
comment wanes. “ The Memoirs of a
Minister consists of short stories written for
serial publication, and though the book con-
taining them is well worth reading it has not
the force of a continuous story. It is un-
pleasant to have one’s interest lifted and
dropped every few pages, and to have to
adapt one’s mind every ten minutes to a
fresh set of persons and circumstances, which
yet pretends to be a familiar set. The matter,
too, though frequently good and strong, tends
on the whole to thinness, and the “Memoirs”
cannot be held equal to the author’s best
work.

THE STORY OF A BABY.
Ward, Lock, and Bowden issue Ethel
Turner’s new book, “ The Story of a Baby,”
as the first number of their new Nautilus
Library (2s. 9d.) —in size, type, binding, and
finish a paradise of dainty book-makers’
devices.
Add “The Story of a Baby” to “ Seve
Little Australians,” and one feels pret
Bure in saying that Miss Turner’s chie
hteiary characteristic will Drove to be, no
power, but charm. “ The Story of a Baby
is an extremely charming book, with am
amount of intuition and sympathy, and
good deal of careful observation, but fallin
short in the matter of insight and reality.
This must not be misconstrued: “ Th
Story of a Baby” does not fall short of i
aim—only of the impossible ideal kept fo
purposes of very special criticism. Bu
when Larrie and Dot fall out—they are twc
very young married people, and they fall ou
so frequently—Larrie is always unconvincin
-—to a male. His wife is an aggravatin
butterfly-girl, meant to spread prett
wings in the sunshine, not to make wa
and hive honey, and Larrie gets angr
with her. Larrie is 22, and very foolish
but is he not a little too foolish, too muc
like a dream of the unfettered girlish imagine
tion, when he quotes texts at his wife, parser
fashion, and harangues her schoolmaste:
fashion, and orders her to obey him anc
doesn’t know that he is doing his best t
incite her to disobedience ? It would seen
bo, to a mere male.
Compare the kindred idyll of Davi
Copperfield and Dora. David is sometime
a little vexed with his child-wife—as whe
Trad dies comes to tea and the oysters ar
put on the table unopened—but he doesn
bully her into rebellion. Certainly David i
older, and graver, and sensibler than Larrie
but his conduct seems more probable', ever
allowing for the difference of tempera
ment. And Larrie has brains—at least
he is a solicitor, if that counts ; and h
takes in The Bulletin (a beastly pape
which the servant surreptitiously purloin
and swallows in the intervals of butcher
flirtation) —and he loves his wife ; and hi
wife is about as much to blame a
he is ; so that he cannot altogethe
be classed with Balzac’s monkey who
played the violin. And if he is an
atypical male, is it fair to average males
to let him roam round the Red Road country
as a sample Australian, without drawback o*
proviso ? One answer to this query migh
be that Dickens acted the part of Larrie
rather than of David when he took a Dora
of his own, and that Larrie is really
common object in marital hedge-rows.
(Rhyme with “cows” to get the full flavour).
Another answer, and one eminently more
satisfactory to male prejudice, is that Miss
Ethel Turner is—Miss Ethel Turner, and
will know better some day.
That is the only fault, if it be fault, that
one finds with “ The Story of a Baby.” The
man may have occurred, but he certainly
isn’t normal, or he doesn’t seem normal to a
normal male. He is just an alleged-mascu-
line reduplication of Dot—Dot with a pipe
and trousers. Considering his youth and
temperament and the temptation he had to
make an ass of himself—why, he might have
done it. But then the author should have
persuaded us that he would have done it—
she should have choked the demon of impro-
bability before he had time to utter one cry.
And she hasn’t.
Miss Turner owes a good half of her suc-
cess to her talent for language, her sense of
words, her capacity to picture moods and
tensions with the living glow which they
have in her mind’s eye. No other Austra-
lian storyteller has ever prattled so plea-
santly.
Reference to “Seven Little Australians ”
shows it to still seem so good that one is
doubtful whether “ The Story of a Baby ” is
an advance. If it is, the improvement lies
in better restraint and proportion, better
elisions and omissions.
There is a paragraph (on page 28) which
is too close an imitation of one of Swin-
burne’s beautiful baby-poems. It should be
omitted in next edition.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you crenenfily take,
what are your peculiarities —a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical boolmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

A DISEASE, not a CEIME.
The constant abuse of alcohol fosters a crave—
a crave insatiable and deadly. It deprives valu-
able men of all reason and respect; it drives them
to acts of indecency and crime, and to drink what
their palates may even repel. At this stage
pledges and punishment prove useless, and
physical, as well as mental attention, is urgently
required.
The Orient Institute
has established a wonderful reputation in the
curing of this awful disease, and, during the past
18 months, many prominent men of our colony
have been restored to health, sobriety and
position. Alcoholic stimulants become not only
unnecessary, but, in the majority of cases, un-
palatable. Patients undergo no inconvenience or
restraint, and are free to eat and drink what they
like.

Where the choice is unlimited and the comfort

Our Millinery Lounge,
which ladies say is the most comfortable corner of
its kind in Sydney. At any rate, no pains are
Spared to render it so ; and if convenient settees
and mirrors in abundance can contribute to that
end, then we are on the road to success. Under-
foot is oarpet of neutral tint and accommodating
hue ; above the ceiling is painted cerulean blue,
lending enchantment to the scene. All round are
crystal mirrors, in which lovely bonnets and still
more lovely faces reflect their beauty, giving the
tout ensemble of the surroundings a magic splen-
dour that only nature combined with art can
afford. There is an ever-changing

"Grin and. Bear It."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When we’re feeling rather seedy, and life seems a
gloomy page,
And our trousers are embroidered with the fancy*
work ot age;
When the shades of bygone pleasures throng
around in dim array
And point with ghostly fingers to the thorns upon
the way,
We are bound to meet a party who will give a
pious smirk.
And will say ; “ It’s very shocking; but, old man,
why don't you work ? ”
Then he’ll give his coin a jingle, will observe you’re
growing thin,
And repeat “ It’s very shocking —you must bear it
with a grin ! ”
Oh, the world is chill with shadows, dim with
dark reproach and crime,
And I fain would seek a subject that would shine
in brighter rhyme,
But the heart will not be merry when the stomach’s
fed with air,
And one’s only boon companion is the phantom
form of care.
It has all been sung so often—oh ! the dreary
time of need ;
“ Out of everything ” but hatred of the weary life
we lead.
And we still must live and lead it; but, although
it may be sin,
Though I’ll bear misfortune firmly, I’ll be damned
if I can grin I
Though the present holds no promise, and the
past no pleasure brings,
For “ a sorrow’s crown of sorrow is remembering
happier things,”
Yet I like to have the phantoms of the old life at
my beck,
When I used to meet my troubles —as they meet
me—with a “check ! ” *
When I fancied I had tasted all the bitters of the
cup,
If my tailor failed to fit me, or I lost a “ fifty-up.”
I could then atiord to moralise, in aphorisms thin,
And could take my gruel kindly—just like whisky
—with a grin.
I can grin no more—my brothers, we’ve enough of
manhood left
Not to weakly smile at evils that have left us hope-
bereft 1
There are hosts will do the laughing ; it is fortune s
pet who knows
That the funniest sight in nature is a man in
shabby clothes.
And there’s little use in “ growling,” we can bear
our ills with grace,
But —we need not lick the fingers that have
slapped us in the face.
When the bolts of fortune strike us we will let
him laugh who wins ;
He’s a Man who bears them bravely—he’s a Fool
who feebly grins !
Think it not! tho’ wrong oppress us, and we seek
in vain for light;
It is not by helpless grinning that we’ll triumph
in the fight.
If I laughed at every sorrow I’d be smirking all the
while,
And my cheeks would now be wrinkling in a most
expansive smile.
We may show our front to trouble, and may make
a bow to fate,
But ’tis only saints—and women—who can beam
on those they hate.
And I give this pledge to fortune, be it right or be
it sin,
When she smites me, well, I’ll hear if—but I’m
damned if I can grin !
T. H. WILSON.
Henry Lawson’s book, which may be
called “Block and Backblocks: Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of that sort, will
be out shortly and will, like “ Banjo’s ” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss, 6d., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success. |
Subjoined hunk of eloquence is not from
Jeremiah, but from the mouth of the prophet
Wragge : “Thus it is that factors which have
operated as secondary laws to cause a prolongation
of the dry weather have at length to give way
under the inevitable and standard regime of the
‘lnfinite and Eternal Energy’ from which all
things proceed.” In plain English—it has been
dry and now it’s wet. |

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue. ;
From Rochester (Vic.) Times
In-the case of the young man Clarke, charged with
using had language on Saturday night, although he
pleaded guilty and two constables swore to having warned
him before taking him into custody, two of the magis-
trates discharged him, the third dissenting. This sort of
thing will scarcely encourage the police in the execution
of their duty.
One of the merciful magistrates, it may be re-
marked, keeps a pub.
A young Melbourneite, about to enter the
civil service, was asked for the usual certificate of
birth. Now, he was born on the sea, on an
Australian-bound ship, and, therefore, reckoned
he was born to trouble as the sparks fly upward.
Knowing the ship’s name, he applied to the agents
for help. They advised him to search the news-
papers of tlie time. He did, and found this :
“Arrived s.s. Thingamy, from London, via ports.
Left with 58 passengers ; arrived with 59, one
lady passenger having on the voyage given birth
to a fine girl.” He swore.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKE RS.
WE bee to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American iobaoco Co.,
Ltd of N S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of Allen and
Gintcr’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the,manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THREE CASTLES” and Lt C-A-IPST-A-TsT. 75
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
7 fills’ “ Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HGYDE TODMAN & CO. ( Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, foi
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this .
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE ” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes after
?ear« of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century; to accomplish this ; and as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALIIY they could not afford tc
make the forgoing statement if any doubt existed. DIN SON & SONS who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several brands CON-
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;’ but “YANKEE DOODLE
ranks supreme.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
•which you, may pay 6 y Post Office Order, One Poimd
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with tvhom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed I. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches ( those dealing with Ams-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to he re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 9, 1895.
“Taken from the County Gaol."
The case of Rofe, the Sydney solicitor of
influential family who went wrong, and
was sentenced to five years’ gaol, but was
instantly released on finding £2OOO security
for his good behaviour during the term
stated, has helped still further to convince
the public that there is a special law for the
lawyer and quite a different kind of law for
everybody else. The public was already
assured of this by many previous episodes,
but Rofe’s case is so especially glaring and
is such a conspicuous instance of interference
with the course of justice, that it has driven
the fact more firmly than usual into the pub-
lic mind. Rofe’s troubles began with the
notorious Butler trial of a few months ago,
Butler was—and, presumably, unless he has
been ameliorated by the gentle influences of
stone-breaking, still is—a man of shady
character who wanted to secure a divorce
from his wife. Therefore he hired certain
persons of equally bad character to swear
falsely to acts of immorality on her part, and
conspired with them to blast his wife’s good
name and get rid of her. Rofe was solicitor
to the conspiracy, and conducted the case,
and the court, with a lamentable lack of
perspicacity, implicitly believed the state-
ments of this sinister aggregation, and not
only granted the divorce but committed the
chief witness for the defence—one Johnson,
a brother of Mrs. Butler —for perjury.
Johnson was subsequently convicted and
sent to gaol for three years, and then, just
as the case seemed definitely ended, some
entirely new complications arose.
• • «
By a curious series of accidents the bottom
fell out of a memorable conspiracy. The case
against Butler's much injured wife was
proved to be pure invention in all its de-
tails, and the criminal law decided that
Butler, his private detective, his soli-
citor and other peisons had conspired
together to invent false and perjured
evidence, and to pervert justice as it
is in Botany Bay, and to blast the character
of an innocent woman. Johnson, the wit-
less for the defence, had already been con-
victed, so the cage stands somewhat as
follows : Johnson (it would appear) com-
mitted perjury, not for sordid cash considera-
tions, nor for any personal profit, nor with a
malicious intention to injure anybody, but
with the alrnost-pardonable idea of defend-
ing his sister from a brutal injustice. He
did evil that good might come. Knowing
that unhappy victim of manufactured circum-
stances, his sister, to bo absolutely innocent,
as she ultimately proved to be, but finding
it impossible to prove her innocence by a
strict adhesion to the truth, he tried to meet
perjury on the wrong side with a much less
heinous perjury on the right side.- He
(assuming his guilt, which even now is by no
means clear) proceeded to manufacture
some circumstances in his sister’s favor,
and, as already mentioned, was sentenced
to three years in gaol as’ a conse-
quence. Butler, who was urged on by
his desire to get rid of a woman of whom he
was weary, and thus had some kind of intel-
ligible motive, however corrupt it might be,
got seven years. His accomplices, who sold
themselves for money, and who could plead,
as some kind of extenuating circumstance,
that they were people to whom money was
an important consideration, got three and
seven years respectively. And Rofe, the soli-
citor, whose case had no shadow of extenu-
ating circumstances whatever— who didn’t
want to get rid of a wife of whom he had
grown weary, who came of a wealthy family
and wasn’t pressed for money, and who didn’t
perjure himself for a good motive, like John-
son, but conspired, and by his long-continued
silence shored up the conspiracy, against an
innocent woman, apparently from mere greed
and cold-blooded infamy—was nominally
sentenced to five years in gaol. But as he
was released there and then under the pro-
visions of the First Offenders' Act, the sen-
tence was purely nominal, and he doesn’t
serve even one hour’s imprisonment on
account of his crime. All of which goes to
show once more the advantages of being a
criminal solicitor in New South Wales, as
compared with the disadvantages of being
any other kind of criminal.
* • *
The circumstances under which the release
was granted were as extraordinary as the
release itself. Barrister Heydon, for the
defence, applied that solicitor Rofe should
be set free for these six alleged reasons :
(1) He was a young man, and one of those
to whom the First Offenders Act. was
especially intended to apply ; (2) He* had
been “ led astray ” by a cleverer man than
himself ; (3) He came of a good family ; (4)
The Law Institute would probably take pro-
ceedings in the matter, and cause him to be
suspended or else struck off the rolls ; (5)
The jury was influenced by “other circum-
stances just now in the public mind ” ; and
(6) Attorney-General Want had “ mentioned
that he would be glad if the Judge could see
his way clear to deal with Rofe under the
First Offenders Act.” Which altogether
made six of the most remarkable reasons
ever publicly advanced for the release of a
well-connected solicitor convicted of a heart-
less conspiracy against the honor of an inno-
cent woman.
* # *
The object of the First Offenders Act is
clear and distinct. It was found that the
practice of committing to gaol mere boys and
girls who had been guilty of one trivial
offence was not calculated to lead to their
reformation, but rather rendered all pros-
pect of reformation hopeless. The gaol
stigma made them practically outcasts
for the rest of their lives, and the
association with older criminals made
them, as a rule, incomparably worse
than they had been before. Considering
these results Parliament decided that per-
manent moral and social ruin was too heavy
a penalty in such cases, and consequently
the First Offenders Act came into existence.
But hitherto it has never been applied to
men educated in the law and practising it
as a profession ; it has been applied
almost entirely in cases where one wrong
action was done under sudden tenqitation or
extreme pressure of circumstances—not
where a deliberate, sordid, unprovoked in-
famy had been of many months’continuance.
The purposes of the Act, as carefully defined
the other day by that expert lawyer, Justice
Windbyer, apply to Rofb’s case as
little as they do to that of Dean, who
was also young and inexperienced, and
who conspired to save himself rather than to
destroy some one else. Also, they apply
very much less to Rofe than they do to
Johnson, who at worst committed his crime
out of natural love and affection, to save his
innocent sister—whereas Rofe’s offence was
com mitted to destroy an innocent woman—and
who was directly incited to perjury by the con-
spiracy of which Rofe was a part. Yet the
same Attorney-General Want, who was used
as a means of indirect interference to save
Rofe, is a leading man in the Execu-
tive which lately decided that Johnson
must serve his sentence to the bitter end.
If this is not one law for the lawyer, then
what is it 1 Also, it is worth mentioning
that the merciful Mr. Want, who, on his
own admission, was glad to see lawyer Rofe
go practically unpunished, was the Attorney-
General at Mount Rennie time, when the
last penalty of the law was inflicted upon
four boys (some of them first offenders) of the
average age of 18 years only.
* * m
The law claims to be no respecter of
persons, yet barrister Heydon, according to
the S.M. Herald’s report, advanced, as one
reason for clemency, the fact that Rofe
belonged to a “respectable family.” Pre-
sumably the intention was to ask that the
law should not take quite its usual course in
the case of a man with influential relations.
If there was no such object in view, then
there was no more reason for mentioning
that Rofe was well connected than there
was for stating that he wore No. 6 boots, or
that he had a gold-stopped back tooth ; and
if there was such an object in view, then
the plea was a tolerably straight request
that justice should be specially lenient to the
rich and well-connected criminal. The state-
ment that Rofe had been led away by
Butler was mere assumption. The state-
ment that he had suffered great mental
anguish is a mere truism; every criminal
suffers mental anguish when he is found
out (Butler made no admission until
he had been practically bowled out),
but ordy the influential criminal has his
anguish deducted from the sentence. And
the assertion that there were “other cir-
cumstances just now in the public mind ”
was a still weaker platitude, and it cuts two
ways. The other three conspirators were
tried under the same circumstances. There
are always other circumstances in the
public mind, and no man has ever yet
had the advantage of being tried when the
public mind was wholly empty of circumstances
of some unpleasant kind or other. Even
the statement that' the Law Institute would
probably deal with the erring solicitor was
utterly and stupidly irrelevant. In the first
place, the court had nothing whatever to do
with what the Law Institute might or might
not do at some future date. And, even if the
Institute has procured the utter abolition of
Rofe in his legal capacity, he thereby suffers
no more than any other well-advertised
offender who doesn’t belong to the legal pro-
fession. When a certain Sydney politician,
whom it will be charity not to name, was
sentenced for complicity in the doings of the
Australian Banking Co., he wasn’t formally
struck off any roll, but he was as completely
obliterated as if he had been. Other men of
less capacity, and probably of far less honesty,
make a large income as bank-directors, and
sit in Parliament, and become Ministers of
the Crown, but that politician, in all his
capacities, has gone under utterly. The
same inexorable rule applies to thousands of
men—to every man, whether of high or low
financial standing, who is convicted of dis-
honesty, or even of calamitous reckless-
ness, in any shape. It even applies
without conviction. One Southern poli-
tician, foremost in his day in politics and
finance, was, though no criminal charge was
ever laid against him in connection with his
miserable bank and his other disastrous insti-
tutions, silently and tacitly struck off the
roll, as a man whose career was ended. He
was a rich banker, an influential politician,
and successively held the highest of offices,
yet, in all these capacities, he was quietly
obliterated. The sympathy which is invoked
for the criminal solicitor who is struck off
the roll, as if his case was one of especial
hardship, is mere drivel. He only, at worst,
shares the common fate.
* * *
Probably the most unfortunate feature,
however, in a case which is full of unfor-
tunate features, is the indirect, even if unin-
tentional, interference of Attorney-General
W ant with the course of justice. Mr. Want
is a leading member of the Ministry which
has the power to make Mr Backhouse a
permanent, instead of merely an acting
Supreme Court Judge. Judge Backhouse’s
reputation is that of a man of absolute recti-
tude, but he is presumably only human after
all, and when barrister Heydon informed
his (acting) Honor, though not in so many
words, that the strong-willed functionary,
who may be able, if he pleases, to exercise
such an important influence on Judge
Backhouse’s fortunes, approved of Rofe’s
release, he did it, presumably, with the idea
that Mr. Want’s opinion would exercise
some sort of influence on the Judge’s deci-
sion. If he didn’t do it with that idea, then
there is no visible reason why he should
have done it at all. And it is utterly inde-
fensible that any outside person’s opinion
should be used for such a purpose, or that
because the rich criminal can afford to hire
an influential barrister who has friendly access
to the Attorney-General, he should thereby
gain any advantage, either real or apparent,
over the poor one whose cheap attorney
never associated with that important per-
sonage. Moreover, though it is safe to
assume that Acting-Judge Backhouse was
not consciously influenced by this expression
of opinion (which the Attorney-General
avers was a private opinion and not intended
to reach the ears of the Judge), yet at all
events he did promptly release the offender
as suggested, and that, too, while telling him
that he had committed a “very grave
offence,” and, almost in so many words, that
he had been guilty of perjury as well as con-
spiracy. The Crown Law Office has piled up
charge upon charge against other criminals.
Does it consider that Rofe has perjured him-
self, and, if so, does it think he should
escape prosecution for that crime ? In this
connection, it is notable that Dean has been
prosecuted not only for conspiracy, bub for
perjury.
* # *
But Attorney-General Want’s action
in expressing the opinion which was used in
this remarkable fashion was even more
unfortunate than the use that was made of it.
The Attorney-General, by virtue of his
office, represented the prosecution, and his
conduct in thus furnishing weapons for the
defence was most unhappy. The state-
ment that his interview with the defendant’s
barrister was private and not offici i! is lame,
for a private conversation simply means that
Mr. Heydon and Mr. Want met together and
talked ; an official conversation means also
that Mr. Heydon and Mr. Want met
together and talked. The best possible
photograph of their meeting couldn't show
any difference between an official interview
and the other kind; and when the Attorney -
General, the representative of the prosecu-
tion, meets the representative of the defence
in a case then proceeding, and talks about
the case, it is hardly possible to be wholly
unofficial. Furthermore, it must be remem-
bered that Mr. Want doesn’t administer the
First Offenders Act, which duty, as he
points out, lies wholly in the province of the
Judge; therefore his admitted statement
that he had no objection to Rofe’s release
under that Act was of no possible use for
any purpose except the one for which Mr,
Heydon naturally used it. Therefore, even
though Mr. Want didn’t make the
ment for that purpose, the only one which
it could possibly be used for, the public Will
probably fail to appreciate the nice distinc-
tion, and the administration of justice will
be discredited accordingly.
* * *
If Rofe had been tried along with his
three fellow-conspirators (that hre was not
tried with them was a mere accident) he
would presumably have had to serve at
least part of his sentence ; even Botany Bay
justice could hardly, when four criminals
convicted of the same conspiracy stood in a
row in the dock, have had the gall to let the
well-connected offender go altogether, and
send the other three, who were not well-
connected, to gaol; besides, how could any
Judge, after measuring Roee’s crime as
deserving of five years, let him go free,
while sending to gaol for her full term
Rofe’s female co-conspiratpr, whose offence
has been rated at three years only 1 The
same process of arithmetic applies to
Johnson’s case. The law, by sentencing
Johnson to three years and Rofe to
five, has deliberately put it on record
that the latter is much the worse criminal
of the two, and the dull, illogical public
naturally wants to know if the reason
why the smaller offender isn’t released while
the greater one goes free is that the latter is
well-connected and a lawyer, while the
former is neither. But it desires to know
this gently and in a low tone. For
by the peculiar workings of the party sys-
tem the Attorney-General, who holds a semi-
judicial office, is a member of the Ministry,
and the country can’t ask this question too
loudly because, if it did, it would pull down
the Ministry, and wreck the land and in-
come-tax, and the civil service reform
scheme, and Foreign-trade, and the Bogan
bridge at Monkey, and the forest-thinning
farce, and many hundreds of other things
in one act, and make W. J. Lyne Premier of
N.S. Wales. And to avoid these things
Johnson will probably have to rot in gaol,
and his sister die of a broken heart in the
hospital, and Rofe, the well-connected con-
spirator, go free, without much question.
Party Government is a weird and awful
affair.
Some More About the Rofe Case.
Were the same sort of justice served out to
the rascally solicitor Rofe as has been given
poisoner Dean, the former would be proceeded
against on a charge of perjury. In sentencing
Rofe, Acting-Justice Backhouse said : “ In
the witness-box, whenever you found yourself
in a difficulty, instead of owning that you were
wrong, you very often invented something
which was transparently untrue. I believe
your own evidence had more to do with your
conviction than anything else.” Is Rofe to be
prosecuted (like Dean) for perjury? If not,
why not? Will Attorney-General Want ex-
plain ?
« « *
Mr. M'Gowen, M.L.A., the supposed leader
of New South Wales Labor party, has done
himself no particular good politically by ex-
pressing his approval of the liberation of the
convicted attorney Rofe under the First
Offenders’ Act. It is a coincidence that Rofe re-
sides in Mr. M‘Gowen’s constituency, and is one
of the shining lights of a local church, while
M'Gowen runs a Sunday-school close by. Is
the Rofe release Labor-party justice ? What
say the Labor electors ?
* • «
Rofe was most diplomatically defended. In
the first place, he had the invaluable services of
Mr. Barton, Q.C., than whom no man is more
effective and popular with juries, and his turn
other counsel were Roman Catholics—it appa-
rently being a well-recognised rule of legal
strategy not to obtrude your own “ color ” upon
the twelve good men and true.
• # *
It cannot be said that the Crown unduly
pressed the case against Rofe. All through
prosecutor Wade’s address it could be seen that
he was prepared to give the accused the benefit
of any fair doubt, and his cross-examination of
the prisoner was not at all severe. Judge
Windeyer was not called in re the interview
between himself and accused in May last, when
Rofe admitted that the co-respondent in the
divorce-case was Butler’s paid servant. Why
was his Honor’s ev.deuce not taken?
• • #

following Davis’s arrest at Mrs. Butler’s
instigation. Also Rofe, according to his own

* * *
This is the way to get a clear view of the
unspeakable outrage on true justice committed
in releasing Roee. It was by pure accident
that Rofb was not tried with the other three
conspirators : that he was not so tried is due to
the fact that his guilt only became apparent
during the course of their trial. Suppose that
all four conspirators had been put into the
same dock, and tried and convicted and sen-
tenced together, how could Judge Backhouse
then have ventured to say in effect—“ Butler,
you scoundrel, go to gaol for seven years. Davis,
you ruffian, seven years for you, too. Roee,
poor young attorney, your share is five
years, but you won’t have to serve it.
I will not keep you in torture : you
may leave the dock on your respectable family,
or someone else, finding £2OOO security for
your good behaviour. As for you, Elizabeth
Peake, though you are a woman and this is
your first conviction, you will have to go to
gaol for three years.” At such a grotesque
spectacle the whole earth would have howled
with derision.
* • *
And why should this identical thing now take
place by reason of the mere fact that Roee,
convicted of the same crime, was tried
separately from his fellow-conspirators?
* * *
If ex-attorney Roee is really sorry for the
unspeakable wrong he has helped to inflict on
Mrs. Butler, and not merely sorry at being
found out, he, or the friends who were so in-
terested in him as to at once find the £2OOO
security necessary to obtain his liberation,
might consider the expediency of taking
measures to substantially compensate the un-
fortunate victim of conspiracy (now in the
Alfred Hospital, Sydney), who, with her two
children, is absolutely destitute. If, years
hence, Roee applied to the Supreme Court for
mercy, evidence of a little practical contrition
on his part at the time of Ins conviction would
much help him both with the Judges and the
public.
» # *
It is pointed out that last month a youth
between 17 and 18 years was tried before Judge
Gibson at Narrabri, and convicted of stealing
from the post two £lO-notes. The envelope
had been left open, and the boy, seeing the
notes, yielded to temptation. In extenuation
and as a reason why accused should get the
benefit of the First Offenders Act, his solicitor
pointed out that he was the main support of a
poor and widowed mother, and that the bulk
of the money stolen had been devoted to paying
off the arrears of rent of his mother’s cottage.
It was his first offence ; his general good char-
acter was deposed to by his chief, the local
postmaster ; by the local P.M., the local In-
spector of I’olice, and others. But Judge Gib-
son declared that the youth was not a fit sub-
ject for tue First Offenders’ Act; his offence
was far too serious.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A British Syndicate.
According to cable advices, some British specu-
lators propose to form a chartered co. to take
over the Northern Territory and run it on the
same lines as those of the co. which bosses
Matabeleland and other regions in South Africa.
The Northern Territory is at present adminis-
tered by South Australia, but its status is not
very well defined. It was “temporarily ceded”
to that luckless province a good many years ago,
but no definite arrangement was ever arrived
at whereby the temporary cession could be
made permanent. South Australia has supplied
the territory at great cost with roads and rail-
ways (such as they are), telegraphs, mail service,
police protection, artesian wells, public schools,
&c., and been a father to it generally to the
best of its ability, and has paid up nearly a
million of the territory’s revenue deficiencies.
Also, the territory sends two members to S.A.
Parliament, and is, to all intents and purposes,
a part of that province. Nevertheless, South
Australia has no absolute legal title to it, and,
under these conditions, the British syndicate,
with the alleged concurrence of Colonial-Secre-
tary Chamberlain, contemplates getting pos-
session of it in one huge grab, and working
it on the nigger and Chinese principles
commonly applied to such cases. On
these principles it could doubtless be
made a highly-profitable speculation—for the
company. A large Asiatic population could be
run into the Territory, and with cheap semi-
slave-labor an enormous amount could be done
in the way of mining, sugar, cotton, rice, and
tobacco culture, &c. The exact benefit which
this country would derive from these opera-
tions, however, doesn’t appear. South Aus-
tralia could also settle the Northern Territory,
if it pleased, with an immense Chinese, Jap.
and nigger population on its own account. It
has only to abolish all restrictions on
the yellow immigrant, and the Chow and
the other varieties of cheap leprosy will
come in like an rtnchained flood, and pro-
ceed to grow almost everything which the
province is capable of producing, and dig for
every mineral which its soil contains. In
short, if it wants to make the Territory a
nigger province, and lay the foundations of a
future racial war, and have lepers and idolaters
and loathsome epidemics slopping over into the
adjacent provinces, it can achieve its desire at
any time, and get for itself whatever profit
there may be to make up for these inconveni-
ences. The only reason why it hasn’t developed
the Northern Territory on these lines is that
no profit, however large, would be worth buy-
ing at the price. The British shindy kit idea
is a fine example of British ignorance, or
bombast, or something of the kind. What
Britain’s claim to the Territory may be is diffi-
cult to define. It never occupied, or explored,
or in any' way developed the country or did
anything whatever for it; all that has been
done is at the cost of Australia. ihe
cession to S.A. was only a temporary ex-
pedient, but, though no definite arrangement
was made for its permanent cession to the Ade-
laide Government, it is equally true there was
no definite arrangement for its occupation by
Britain at any future time. And whatever the
paper-rights of the British Government may be,
any attempt to set up in Australia a syndicate-
governed province, peopled by niggers and
Chinamen, and ruled on British commercial
principles, will mean trouble. The pity is that,
for want of a strong Federal Government, there
is no authority which can make as much trouble
as the occasion calls for.
The Divine Right of Sweaters.
Melbourne Metropolitan Board of Fitz Gibbon
decided some time since, by a small majority,
on the minimum wage. President Fitz Gibbon
fought the motion tooth-and-nail and has since
nullified it by its method of administration. It
is against rules now to sweat wages on Melb.
sewerage works, but the amount of the penalty
is fixed at the amount of the unlawful deduc-
tion made by the contractor, the penalty being
payable to the Board and not to the workman
sweated. The workman now knows that com-
plaint will be followed by dismissal, and
that the contractor will pay the fine
with a smile and recoup himself a hundred-
fold by continuing his oppression of the
poor. Fitz Gibbon readily fashions an appro-
priate sophistry. “The man who engages to
accept less than the minimum wage, and subse-
quently complains of his treatment, is dis-
honest. I will be no party to paying
that man the balance which he would receive
under the minimum wage, as such an act would
be giving a premium to dishonesty.” Dis-
honesty in the sweated is hideous, but dis-
honesty in the sweater is to be met by a
paltry fine, a mere license-fee to sweat. The
criminal who complains against? sweating gets
dismissal for his pains. The criminal who
sweats is allowed to continue as a contractor,
and his abominable practices are made a source
of revenue to the Board. Mr. Fitz Gibbon is
recommended to one of his own prescriptions
for crime. As one of the principal snuffle-
busters of the National Scripture Education
League, he once made these remarks : —■
The State’s first duty is the prevention of crime. No
man could forge unless he first learnt to write ; no secret
poisoning could be effected except by a highly-educated
man [such as Dean—Ed.]. What was to counteract this
effect? Nothing but the teaching of The Book, which the
State excluded.
The Bulletin recommends Fitz Gibbon and A.
E. Moore and one or two other choice spirits on
the Board to wrestle in prayer with the
sweaters and the sweated on the sewerage
contracts, and they can take their Bibles wdth
them as an effective cure for crime, with the
marker in at the following passages :
Behold the hire of the laborers . . . which is of
you kept back by fraud, crieth.
I will be a swift witness against those that oppress the
hireling in his wages, saith the Lord of hosts.
If I did despise the cause of my muinservant or of my
maidservant when they contended with me ; what then
shall Ido when God riseth up? And when He visiteth
what shall I answer Him ?
Fitz Gibbon howled piteously when his salary
was reduced to .£3B a week, saying something
higher was stipulated, and the Board unanim-
ously agreed with him. But when a wretched
laborer, sweated down to 12s. fid. or 15s. a
week—a wage to which his poverty and not his
will consented—complains, he is branded by
the bloated Fitz. as dishonest, and the Board
stands complaisant by. Fitz Gibbon and his
Board are become intolerable. They are a
perpetual insult to a democratic sense of
decency. They seem to represent nobody but
Fat Man, and they certainly oppress everybody
but Fat Man. They justify the wicked for
reward, they oppress the hireling in his wages,
they grind the faces of the poor. They are all
that the pious Fitz. and his sophistry and their
own pride and prestige can make them, and
they will reap the blessed damnation awaiting
those who drag iniquity with the cart-rope of
vanity.
The Gentle Kanaka.
Editor Bulletin, —Ere the sensation aroused
by the murder of a white man near Bundaberg
(Q.) by a gang of docile Kanakas subsided,
the mouldering corpse of a blackfellow was
found lying in a field of sugar-cane. The body
had been hacked by the tomahawks and knives
of the “cheap and reliable” laborers who
support Queensland’s staple industry. This
murder was the forerunner and cause of the
cold-blooded killing of the white man Lawty
on Oct. 19. The victim of the crime was one
Tobgo, a Malaytan, and about six weeks prior
to his body being found he was returning from
town to his plantation with two other islanders.
He lagged behind them, and was not
afterwards seen until his body was found,
top-dressing a planter’s cane-field. His fellow
Malaytans concluded that he had been killed by
Gala islanders, many of whom are employed on
a neighboring plantation. Rows between the
two tribes followed, and culminated in the
murder of Lawty, who was mistaken by the
assassins on a dark night for a Gala “boy.”
These facts are established by the confession of
one of the murderers. The supporters of the
slave-trade (a motion for the abolition of which
has now been tabled by Drake, M.P.) seek to
wriggle out of the dilemma in which these
murders have placed them, and by proposals
which are as brutal as they are absurd.
The Bundaberg council at last meeting
carried a resolution calling on Government
to restrict the Kanakas from visiting
town after sundown. This means that the gilt
is to be taken off the Polynesian gingerbread,
and that the docile Kanaka—thealleged Messiah
of the Q. sugar industry—is to be treated as a
slave pure and simple* It further means that
the planter who owns him and who runs a store
on the plantation will get back all the nigger’s
wages for inferior goods retailed at fancy prices
But it means still more than this. It will place
the country residents, whose dwellings are few
and far between, at the mercy of the planters
pets, and compel them also to remain at home
at night —and barricade their dwellings, xhe
consequences of keeping thousands of stalwart
adult male savages, supplied only with a few
score of their female countrywomen, out
of the town where alone their animal
propensities are gratified can be imagined.
Out of evil good will come in these cases. The
people are ominously growling and calling for
the abolition of the kanaka-evil altogether, and
events seem to be playing into the public hands.
To such a pass have things come in this Bunda-
berg district that, during the last six months,
no fewer than 30 or 40 white men have been
sent to gaol for supplying kanakas with drink.
The niggers give them the money and a com-
mission to buy the drink, and the starvmg
wretches, who have no chance of getting work,
risk three months’ gaol for the sake of earning
sixpence commission from a nigger wherewith
to buy a feed. Apropos , a few months ago
a gaily-attired demi-mondaine was charged
in the police court with disorderly conduct as a
■“ common prostitute.” She admitted the dis-
orderly conduct, but indignantly repudiated
the character given her. “I am not a
‘ common ’ prostitute,” she said. “I have
dealings with the kanakas only. A Mean
White.
The N.S.W. Council on the Land-Tax.
The N.S.W. Legislative Council, at latest
advices, was still hacking the Land and Income-
tax Bill out of all semblance of its original
shape. Up to time of writing the Land-tax
exemption has been wholly struck out; the
clause prohibiting the landlord passing the tax
on to his tenant has been obliterated; the
absentee-tax is abolished; the income - tax
exemption is reduced to .£l6O instead of £3OO
as proposed by the Assembly ; the provision
for State resumption of land at 10 per cent,
over the owner’s valuation has gone the way of
the Land-tax exemption ; and, instead of the
mortgagee coming under the Land-tax provi-
sions 7 the interest he receives is to be reckoned
as ordinary income and to pay income-tax.
This latter is almost the only alteration that
the Leg. Council has yet been guilty of which
is, in any sense, an improvement. It saves
much intricate and cumbrous arithmetic, and
as the tax will be calculated not on the amount
lent, but on the interest charged, it follows
that, instead of all lenders being taxed alike,
the usurer who charges 15 per cent, will be
required to pay just three times as much as
the man who charges only five, which is
a system that cannot be too highly commended.
This is the good aspect of the case, but it pro-
bably isn’t the aspect which the Council had in
view. The other side of the story is that,
assuming the land-tax to be Id. in the <£, and
the income-tax 6d. in the £, which are the
generally-accepted figures, the mortgagee, under
the new system, will pay, if his interest is 8
per cent., less than half as much as he would
under the system embodied in the Bill as it
passed the Assembly, while at 5 per cent, he
will pay less than one-third. This opens up a
way for sundry profitable swindles, one of
which would work something like this. The
registered owner of a mortgaged estate will
necessarily pay land-tax only on the proportion
of the property which he actually owns over
and above the mortgage. The mortgagee,
instead of paying land-tax, will pay income-tax
on the amount which he receives annually as
interest. There-fore, if the land-tax is levied, as
is generally anticipated, at Id. in the £ on the
capital value, and the income-tax at 6d. in the
£ of income, Fat Man may profit very consider-
ably. If he owns an unimproved property
valued at £120,000, the land-tax thereon will
be ,£5OO per annum. But if he transfers this
to his son or to his brother Bill, subject to a
mortgage of £lOO,OOO at 5 per cent., which
burden will effectually prevent Bill making
away with the property, the impost will be :
land-tax nominally payable by brother Bill at
Id. in the £ on £20,000, £B3 6s. Bd. ; income-
tax at 6d. in the £ on £5OOO a year interest on
the alleged mortgage, £125 ; total, £2OB 6s. Bd.
This means a saving of very nearly £3OO
a year, or three - fifths of the entire lia-
bility. If the interest is set down at 3 per
cent, the whole amount of the tax will be £l5B
6s. Bd. instead of £500; and if the mortgagee
reports that the interest is irrecoverable, the tax
will be almost nil. The small landowner will
gain very little advantage by this device, partly
because he is, as a rule, almost wholly un-
acquainted with the law, and partly because,
on a small transaction of the sort, the legal ex-
penses of the transfer and the mortgage would
make a serious inroad on the profit. But the
large Fat Man, if he is anything like the Fat Man
he used to be, will profit by it extensively, and
the result will be that he will be enabled to
dodge the largest part of his share of the tax
while the small holder pays up in full. Another
result will be that mortgagees w'on’t foreclose
openly on the land when the principal and in-
terest are overdue; instead of becoming
nominally the owners that they really are,
they will transfer the property to the same use-
ful brother Bill, and remain ostensibly
only mortgagees as before. Still another
result will be that, even apart from
all fraud, a large proportion —probably one-
third—of the anticipated proceeds of the new
impost will disappear altogether. The abolition
of the absentee-tax is apparently mere cussed-
ness, as the legislators of the Council are not
absentees—at all events, not at present. The
abolition of the land-tax exemption and the
reduction of the income-tax exemption are
apparently done to increase the number of tax-
payers, and consequently the number of people
who will have an interest in abolishing the tax,
and to wreak a poetic vengeance on many
people, including the members of Assembly,
who voted for the tax under the impression that
it would only apply to the other man. But the
amendment of the mortgagee-tax is a harmless-
looking provision, which seems as if it didn’t mean
anything at all, and yet may mean a very great
deal. Yet, with all this, there is one amendment
made by the Council which should have unreserved
approval. T t has wholly exempted mutual-life
assurance societies from the operation of the
Income-tax, so that they will pay Land-tax
only. It is a pity they were not exempted from
Land-tax also. Considering how many exemp-
tions the churches have got, this is only fair ;
the life-assurance and the soul-assurance insti-
tution should stand on the same level. In fact,
if there is any difference, the former should have
the preference. The life-assurance institution
generally pays up its engagements in full at
death ; whether the soul-assurance concern pays
its engagements at death no one has ever yet
ascertained.
The periodical deputation of Presbyterian par-
sons (with the New Hebrides Co., most of whose
shareholders live in Sydney, at their backs)
waited the other day on Premier Reid and asked
him to use his best efforts to keep the French out
of the New Hebrides, and the Premier, instead
of throwing the deputation down the stairs, as
he should have done, weakly promised to
hustle round in the matter. The New Hebrides
are fully 1100 miles away from New South
Wales, and about 1000 miles away from the
nearest part of Australia. They lie on the
other side of New Caledonia, so while France
has New Caledonia, the annexation of a few
small islands beyond it can’t make the
danger of French aggression any worse than
it already is. They are further away than
the Dutch and German territories in New
Guinea, and they are of no earthly importance to
New South Wales and of very little to Aus-
tralia in general. But the New Hebrides are a
convenient preserve for the Presbyterian mis-
sionaries, about twenty of whom make an easy
living by telling the local brown savage about
the advantages of hell. If the French annex
the miserable little group, then the R.C.
fathers will come and tell the brown man about
hell instead, and herein lies the beginning and
middle and end of the whole trouble. It is a
poor, mean, sordid scramble after the loaves
and fishes and cocoa-nuts, and Australia, which
has no interest in the matter, p Ljos»ld let the
respective parties struggle for them the best
way they can.
The Social Gimlet Brigade will have a good
time in Tasmania if a certain bill for the pro-
tection of children becomes law. This measure
provides that any policeman, or any other
“authorised person,” may enter the house of
any “reputed drunkard or prostitute,” and
haul any children found therein before a magis-
trate, with a view to having them sent to an
industrial school. All the parsons, and all the
aggressive teetotallers, and all the old pious
gorgons of both sexes intend to be authorised
persons under this bill, if they can manage it.
Then, as no one can define what “ reputed ”
really means, they will be at liberty to raid the
house of every person whom they suspect to
smell of drink, on the ground that he is a
reputed drunkard, and every poor woman
whose means of livelihood—as explained by her
to the intruding old person of the W.C.T.U.—
do not appear sufficiently definite. The Tas.
Parliament should insert a proviso in the bill,
w'hile there is yet time, that no parson or
teetotal-lecturer, or member of the W.C.T.U.,
or the Y.M.C.A., or the Y.W.0.A., or the
Social Purity Society, and no bounding brother
or sister of any sect whatever, shall be an
“authorised person.” If it doesn’t, there will
be a great multiplication of the interfering old
women who love to tramp six abreast into
another person’s home, and tell her that, unless
she can give a satisfactory account of herself, it
will be their painful duty to “take steps.”
According to S.M. Herald’s report of Judge
Backhouse’s remarks when sentencing attorney
Roee, His Honor said :
The sentence of Che court is that you, Thomas Ernest
Rofe, be imprisoned and kept to hard labor in Her
Majesty's Gao) at Darlinghurst for five years; that sen-
tence to be suspended under the provisions of the First
Offenders’ Act for two reasons. First of all, for what has
been said about the Attorney-General, because I am glad
that the Attorney-General’s view is the same as mine ;
but the responsibility rests entirely with me,” &c.
The plain, ordinary meaning of this is that
the Attorney-General’s expression of opinion,
brought to the ears of the Judge, was
one of two reasons which influenced him
to suspend the sentence. Does Judge Back-
house admit the correctness of this report ?
Does he deny it ? If it is correct, does he think
his administration of justice should be influ-
enced by the remarks of the Attorney-General
(who is, after all, only one of two parties
to the suit Reg. v. Rofe) whether
made intentionally or, as alleged in this
case, in the course of a private conversation
not meant to be divulged to the court ?
Are Judges in future to ascertain which way
the Attorney-General’s wind blows, before
making up their minds? Suppose Attorney-
General Want had thought Rofe ought to go
to gaol, would Judge Backhouse have con-
sidered one of his “reasons” gone, and sent
Rofe to gaol ? There have been Judges on the
Australian Bench who, informed during the
progress of a case that the Attorney-General
had expressed this or that hope about the sen-
tence, would have d d the Attorney-General
so effectually that the retailer of his conversa-
tions would have shivered for the rest of his
natural life at the mere recollection.

Alfred Dampier re-appeared at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday as the picturesque
Starlight, in “Robbery Under Anns,” and a
crowded house gave him such a reception as
hardly anyone ever got there before. No one
fully realised the full value of this fine actor and
kind-hearted gentleman until he took his depar-
ture, a year or two ago. When he re-appeared on
Saturday, and his magnificent voice filled the
furthest recesses of the big theatre, the public
rose and stated truthfully, with its feet and
hands and stick and the end of its umbrella, that
Dampier occupies a place on the Australian stage
which no other man at present in this country can
fill. The character of B ddrewood’s idealised
bushranger suits him admirably, and, from
beginning to end, his performance is all that
could be asked for. To be bush ranged in Dam-
pier’s gentlemanly style would be a pleasure and
a privilege ; there is a culture and finish about it
which suggests being bailed-up in Greek verse,
or burgled in absolutely select Sanscrit, or vio-
lently assaulted in Latin. Dampier looks no older
than he did when last seen in Sydney ; that is to
say, he looks about 30 or so, and, if he were con-
victed before a near-sighted Judge, he would
probably be let off under the First Offenders’
Act, on account of his extreme youth.
After Dampier, the best feature of the
drama is the aboriginal of Alfred Woods—
a small and inhumanly active nigger, who does a
lot of miscellaneous service, and bounds like an
india-rubber fiend through five long acts. His
dialogue is scanty, but his bounding is powerful
and highly finished. Miss Lily Dampier’s fine
horsemanship helps out the part of the bush-
ranger’s best girl materially. Mr. Plimmer looks
a lofty and beautiful inspector of police, and
Messrs. Rolfe, Leston, Lachlan M'Gowan and
Haybittle, and Misses Elliott and Katherine
Russell, and a large number of horses, all contri-
bute to keep the show going in various ways of
more or less importance. But the interest centres
mainly round Dampier, and Woods’s aboriginal,
and the scenery, which is particularly good, and
the four horses which are rescued from the burn-
ing stable. A subtle odour of genuine Australian
horse pervades the entire drama in fact, and there
is a faint perfume of gum-tree and wattle right
through, which is eminently pleasing, and a heavy
shower of real rain is thrown in as a guarantee of
good faith,
* * * *
“Old Kentucky ” has settled down to be
an eminently moderate success at Sydney Lyceum.
For some reason or other American drama is
seldom more than a moderate success in the land
of the wattle, and this particular drama is so pro-
foundly moderate in every respect that its impor-
tation is unaccountable. A remarkably strong
company has, so far, prevented its dropping with
a sickening thud into the place where it ought to
be, but each night it seems more and more of a
pity that so much ability and money, and good
painting, and valuable horse, should be wasted
upon it. Elton and Miss Helen Dauvray are
worthy of better things, and the spectacle of Scot
Inglis wandering round pointing a futile gun at
the hero, yet never shooting that sketchy person,
can only be explained on the assumption that the
gun isn’t loaded. Also, King Hedley has little to
do except be a pallid countenance in the midst of a
jungle of black whiskerand stand around generally.
A villain who does hardly anything in view of the
audience, save be recognised as the person who
killed somebody 20 years before, is not active
enough for this great country. A general want of
energy, and a scarcity of plot, and a lack of inci-
dent, and a gentlemanly slowness in the last three
acts are the chief objections to “ Old Kentucky.”
* * * *
Lawler’s Sydney Music Hall opened on
Saturday, before the paint was quite dry, with a
very respectable programme and" a packed audi-
ence. A number of good vocalists and a fine
orchestra are points in the show, and Horace
Bent, whose murky countenance shines on theo.p.
side, has lost none of his moist and fruity humor,
and has accumulated a lot of new jokes during his
prolonged absence from the stage. Horace appears
to improve with age. The ballet of 12 girls all at
once is a pretty and lavish item ; and the four
acrobats (the Leopold Brothers) are remarkably
proficient in their own rather monotonous line of
business. Someone with an intelligent eye to legs
has selected the ladies of the company, and among
the 14 or 20 of them who dance, there isn’t one
that doesn’t dance well. Also, most of them are
sufficiently good-looking to pass inspection any-
where. If the new enterprise keeps on as it has
begun it ought to fill a long-felt want, or some-
thing to that effect.
* * * *
Herr Hajek, the Austrian man of muscle,
has been added to the Tivoli programme this
week. Hajek is an incredible person who places
himself across two chairs, in a neck-and-heels
fashion, and balances an anvil on his chest, and
hires men to pound furiously on it with sledge-
hammers. This is not the kind of amusement
which any ordinary citizen would care for, but
Hajek apparently enjoys it. When ho is out of a
billet he generally accepts a temporary engage-
ment as a steam crane, and he does a little in the
way of shoving off stranded ships. He is also
good at forest-thinning, as he lifts the trees out by
the roots. Some airy proceedings with cannon-balls
and similar small trash which he perpetrates at the
livoli are not meant to be taken seriously ; when
Hajek is in a serious mood he never lifts anything
below a ton. Like most men of stupendous
muscle, he is a soft-hearted person and easily
moved by a tale of woe. and in these moments of
emotion he generally goes out and carries an iron
safe several times round a paddock to calm him-
self. Altogether Hajek is an interesting person.
* * * *
The memorable spectacle with the brown
legs in it is still doing well at Sydney Royal,
where it will probably hold its own for some time.
There is nothing new to report about this stupen-
dous dazzle at present. The Fitzgeralds’ show
leaves at the end of the present week, Mahomet
and the Professor and his tank, and the
Roumanian lady, and the Altons, and the rest of
the clever co., having urgent engagements else-
where. It is reported on bad authority that the
Roumanian charmer has received and rejected
sundry offers of marriage from influential johnnies
during her stay in Sydney. The valor of the man
who wants to many a lady (already married)
capable of falling on him from a height of 140 ft.
must be of the most desperate description.
* * * *
Mahomet, the talking horse, of Fitz-
geralds’ show, after visiting the Australia bar,
without bridle, help, or guidance, and merely at
trainer Probasco’s request, lately walked up
30 steep steps into the studio of Kerry and
Co., the Sydney photographers. Arriving
there he remarked that fifteen ladies were pre-
sent, and that he’d like to embrace all
fifteen. One lady violently responding with a
frenzied wish to hug Mahomet, she and the four-
footed soul kissed each other upon the cheeks.
.The photo, ceremony over, Mahomet proceeded
home to lunch. Putting tiis head down near the
steps he sagaciously followed his nose with one
foot after the other, and arrived safely in the
street.
* * * *
The excuse for re-opening Melbourne
Alexandra is a five-act horror, which might be
mistaken for the horror now being played at the
Royal, if it were not such a rough caricature of
that previous production. Or, perhaps, the other
horror is a rationalised version of the Haleck
drama; or, possibly, the two awful things were
the work of twin brothers who exchanged ideas as
they went along, in which ease one twin must have
been less drunk than his relative, “ The Broad
Arrow” is supported by a villain with long,
black, oily hair, and a hero whose hair is equally
black, but cropped short and not at all greasy.
Charles Holloway, as a rather humorous convict,
also contributes largely to the support of
his own show. Other members of the cast,
including Oily Deering, Miss Dora Mostyn,
Miss Gresham and George Majeroni are over-
shadowed by a railway-smash. The villain per-
sistently heaps false charges upon the hero, and
Holloway always aids the monotonous young
man to escape, but the remainder of the plot is a
¥m re *' an "^ fc) °f small talk and minor offences.
When a corpse is required to thicken the plot an
unknown convict casually slaughters a warder,
and the body of this harmless intruder is used in
evidence against the wrong person. “ The Broad
Arrow ” is full of surprises that thrill the gallery,
its railway-accident is a blood-stained triumph by
John Hennings, and the easy prices of admission
indicate that Charles Holloway means to fill the
house. Ihe state of the interior on Saturday was
fairly encouraging.
* * * *
Meantime, the Royal flourishes by means
of ‘Man to Man.” The railway-smash in this
instance has unlimited stage-room at its disposal,
and the comic felon seems a more acceptable
monster than his counterpart at the Alexandra.
His frank and open style of wrecking a passenger-
tram in order to pick up a few togs makes him
popular with all classes, and when he is observed
threading his way through the ruins of the awful
railway-calamity, with a portmanteau in each
hand and a happy smile upon his face, the audi-
ence is delighted to think that his efforts were
duly rewarded. George Rignold and his reliable
00. deserve something better than the work of the
less unsober twin, but art is of no great conse-
quence whilst business remains good. “Youth”
will come up for judgment when called upon, next
Saturday or later.

At the Princess’s a kaleidoscopic change of
operas is keeping the company well employed,
fo-night (ihursday) “ 7 fascotte ” occupies the
bill ; to-morrow, “Vicar of Bray,” and on Satur-
day they revive “The Gondoliers.” New tenor,
Courtice Pounds, is an event of the very near

* * * *
Meib. Opera House is lavishly attended at
this festive season, and the twin wheezes of the
ventriloquist fall on good ground. An enduring
feature here is the club-swinging by 15 amazons.
Other items come and go, but the lime-lighted
damsels swing on with a poetry of motion which
never palls.

The Oriental Carnival at Meib. Exhibition
turns out almost better than was expected. The
street in Jerusalem, painted bv artist Harry Grist
is a solid scenic effe t, and the ladies in Eastern np-
parel who haunt that main thoroughfare are dreams
of beauty for the most part. Also, their business
energy in such matters as the raffling of one
golden sovereign among thirty members at a
shilling each deserves great praise, seeing that the
same coin of the realm is always expected to
realise a “6ver” at a church bazaar. Entertain-
ments on a platform under the dome are lively
and frequent, the concert-hall holds out a good
musical programme, and the quadrangle, with
festoons of coloured lamps gleaming upon a bicycle
race or half-mile sprint, is an exceedingly
pretty side-show. Furthermore, a Harem
Theatre for amateur opera performers has been
fixed up near the concert-hall, and dissipations too
numerous to mention are crowded into the tout
ensemble. As a Carnival on its bare merits, apart
from the cause of Jewish charity, which is under-
stood to be interested in the profits, this Carnival
deserves to do well.
Nearly 100,000 human beings are born
every day on to this weary old planet. Every
10 days a fresli million of excited men rush
forth at 2 a.m. seeking for a million nurses in a
million remote suburbs. Every day is, therefore,
the birthday of a hundred thousand somebodies.
Which means that the question, “ What shall we
give as a birthday present ? ” is daily agitating an
incredible number of households dotted over the
globe. In Sydney, the other day, a cold, hard-
headed business man had a birthday, whereupon
his three little children gravely invited him to the
circus ! And he went, and was a better man for
it. But this form of present is not always practic-
able, so when the birthday of one of your friends
comes round, you cannot do better than call at
Mark Fuy’s. A handsome silk blouse, a pair of
French shoes, an elegant sunshade, a set of Winsor
and Newton’s artist’s materials, a pair of choice
vases, a handsome fan—any of these would please
just as well as a circus ticket; and, as to prices,
no house can please you like Mark Foy’s.*
In our advertising columns will be found
full detailed particulars of a great property dis-
tribution scheme, concerning the disposal of a
valuable lot of Sydney city and suburban property
by the Equitable Building Society, Brisbane. For
this purpose the society is issuing 150,000 shares
of the value of £1 each, which carry no liability
whatever to the holder of same. The properties
have been obtained from the Colonial Finance
Corporation, Sydney, by conditional purchase,
and were carefully selected by that corporation.
They comprise a total value of .£150,000. Nearly
all of them are situated in and around Sydney.
The first prize in the distribution consists of exten-
sive business premises in George-st., Sydney, oppo-
site the Commercial Bank of Australia, at present
occupied by Messrs. J. C. Lovell & Co., and two
large wool stores in Kent-st., occupied by Messrs.
Mofflin and Co. Ltd, These properties are valued
at £28,500, and return a net rental of £866 per
annum, which will no doubt be considerably in-
creased as present leases mature. No. 2 prize is a
large property, extending through from Princes-
street to Kent-street, with the extraordinary
depth of 330 ft., on which is erected four houses
and two cottages ; and included in this prize is a
block of land in Clarence-street, adjoining Sydney
Town Hall, the total being valued at £21,000.
There are in all 508 prizes, including, apart from
those already mentioned, a large brickworks at
St. Peters, capable of turning out 100,000 bricks
per week ; a splendid mansion at Heathcote,
with garden and orchard, and 48 acres of land;
a large block of city property in Jamieson-street,
Sydney, together with handsome 10 roomed house
in Ocean-street, Woollahra; several excellent
shop properties in Oxford-street, including Wig-
zell’s Turkish Baths; and other valuable build-
ings in city and suburbs, together with a few in
country districts close to Sydney. One most
important point in this distribution is the fact
that so many of the prizes are rent producing.
The values placed upon the several properties
seem reasonable, and would probably be realisable
even in the present existing state of the market,
but it is considered advisable to offer them
through the medium of a property distribution
so as to secure their more rapid disposal. The
committee of management of the society includes
the well-known names of the Hon. B. B. Moreton,
Hon. John Donaldson, and Acheson Overend,
Esq., while the Sydney Board of Reference
comprises Messrs. Bruce Smith, J. F. Burns, and
W. J, Green, all of whose names are sufficient to
give to the public an undoubted guarantee as to
the distribution being carried out with perfect
bona Jides in an honorable manner. Mr. George
Adams (Tattersall) has placed his complete ballot
apparatus and his services at the disposal of the
committee, and will personally supervise the
drawing, which is in itself a guarantee that no
hitch will occur in this respect. The drawing is
announced to take place on December 20th, 1895,
so that intending shareholders will require to
make early application, as the list will close on
the 17th December, 1895. According to ihe arrange-
ments as advertised each shareholder will have no
less than 30 separate chances, so that an investor of
£1 may receive the amazing dividend of £125,910.
Altogether, the Equitable Building Society’s
distribution seems a thoroughly legitimate specu-
lation, and offers every investor fora very small
sum a fair chance to become a property holder on
a large scale. Shares and all particulars can be
obtained from the secretary of the Equitable
Building Society, 212 Queen-street, Brisbane, or
from the Society’s Sydney agent, Mr. G. C.
Chalmers, 72 Pitt-street, Sydney. The fullest
partieu'ars as to properties wdl be found in the
advertising co’umns of this paper.

Dear Bulletin,— Re “Lola Montes.” According to
Mr. Ryan’s reminiscences the whilom favorite of the
Bavarian monarch arrived in Australia in ’57. The pre-
sent writer saw her perform in ’54—when she danced the
spider dance at Geo. Ooppin’s Theatre Royal, Geelong.
Her figure must have been very willowy, as her stage-
jacket, which the scribe had in his hand, was volumin-
ously padded to give her the semblance of a decent figure.
There are other errors in Mr. Ryan's reminiscences.
The name of the editor whom she horsewhipped was
Sekam, not “Senior,” and the affair did not occur at
Ballarat, but at some other town. A reference to the
files of Geelong Advertiser and Melbourne Argus,
between ’54 and ’56, would settle the matter.- Call Boy.

Dear Bulletin, —The end of that bright amateur
comedian, Jack Stanway, the shipping clerk who was the
tool in those Melb. Custom - house frauds, was a
tragedy indeed. The convicted firm is not much
worse than most other people — hundreds of firms
cook invoices for the Customs—and nearly all firms
have a clerk at £2 or £3 a week or so pulling
the thestnuis out of the fire to the tune of thou-
sands a year. If the clerk refuses to pull the chestnuts
out of the fire, the monkey “ sacks ” him. But that
firm was found out, and its catspaw—who hadn’t
benefited by the frauds—came down the hardest, because
he was the person of least account. Stanway suffered
from a painful internal disease, which was aggravated by
the shook of going to gaol, and the anxiety for a wife and
children left destitute, and when he was committed to
prison he utterly collapsed. He could not w ork, and the
warders, thinking he was another Emanuel Abrahams—
as he was employed by a Hebrew firm they thought he
was a Hebrew—dragged him on to his feet when he had
fallen from sheer wpaKness. Suddenly the medical autho-
rities took alarm and ordered his release, and he went
home to die. Three days later Stanway was buried, his
crazed wife screaming “Goodbye, my Jack!” as the
hearse started for the cemetery. A man dying an
agonised death—a crazed woman—a wrecked home —
because a smart firm swindled the Customs of half
the duly on cheap watches per medium of a clerk,
who bad to help them swindle—or starve. Let the
amateurs of Melbourne help Mrs. Stanway.—Randolph
the Reckless.
