The Wild-Cat Column .
The grand old Bank of New South Wales shows
A net profit for the half-year of £84,787, and the balance
of £21,848 from last half-year-makes the splendid total of
£100,685 available for distribution. A div. at the rate of
9 per cent, per annum and interest at 6 per cent, on calls
paid in advance absorb £84,536, and £22,049 is carried
forward. A sum of £14,225 received as premiums on new
Shares is added to reserve, making it. £1,172,297. Paid-up
capital is now £1,894,200. Tire B.N.S. W. has Hie unprece-
dented sum of £6,731,525 in cash, and over £1,000,000 in
other liquid securities, equal to nearly one-third of its
entire liabilities to the public. The fact that it keeps
this enormous dead weight of coin lying practically idle,
and yet pays a handsome div. and shows no falling away
in the profits, is an awkward commentary on the stereo-
typed explanation put forward by other institutions, that
their decline is not owing to unprofitable investments,
hut to the necessity for keeping large cash reserves which
earn no interest. The balance-sheet of the B.N.S.W. is
decidedly the most satisfactory document of its kind
which Australia has seen in this much-depressed year.

The last Bank of England returns showed the
cash-balance of the British Government, held by that
institution, was £6,722,000 —for a Govt, with a yearly
revenue of £95,000,000 and a population one hundred
times as great as Queensland. Compare the two —
£6,722 000 for a population of 40 millions ; and £4,069,954
for a population of 450 000. Or, as the Courier puts it
“Queensland, with a turn-overof 3| millions per annum,
holds a hank balance of over four millions ; while Great
Britain, witli a turn-over of £95/00,000, holds a hank
balance of six and three-quarter millions.”

The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney has
given one month’s notice of the release of all its extended
deposits. The sum reconstructed by this institution in
’93 was a little over £10,000,000, hut instalments have
been set free at various limes, and the amount of this
final release is only about £1,000,000. The Bank of North
Queensland was the first institution to completely se t
free its reconstructed cash, and the Commercial Banking
Co. is the second. The National Bank of Aust.rala.sia
should certainly he the third. The National, according
to its latest balance-sheet, has a considerably larger pro-
portion of cash to deposit liabilities than the Commercial
Banking Co., and under these circumstances it is difficult
to understand why it should not, follow that institution s
example—except it he that the great bank-scare was most
acute in Melbourne, and the hair of the different financial
institutions stood up so violently through the horror of
that episode that it has never really got down since.
* • • • *
The finances of Maoriland, as expounded by
Treasurer Ward, are arousing consid'rable ill-feeling.
Many years ago Maoriland used to float its loans with a
sinking-fund attached for redemption purposes. For a
while the payments to this fund were made regularly,
and at one lime about a million and-a-half was thus
accumulated. Then the Sfout-Vogel Government passed
an Act whereby the payments to the sinking-fund were
still made, hut the Treasurer was allowed to borrow each
year an amount equal to that which he put into the fund
and the interest received on past accumulations, and use
it as ordinary revenue, or for any other purpose he
saw fit. Most of M.L.’s recent surpluses were
produced by thus practically annexing the annual
payments to the fund, hut still • this process did
not cause any actual increase of the public debt, and so
long as the amounts annexed were us ,j d for public
works only the province was certainly doing very
much better than any of its neighbors. The process
panned out as follows : The Treasurer would pay, say,
£lOO,OOO out of revenue to the sinking-fund ; he would
borrow £120,000, being the amount of the payment and
the interest on the fund, and put it into revenue, then
there would he a surplus of £120,000, and it would be set
aside for railway extension or other like purposes.
This year, however, Treasurer Ward uses
the sinking-fund money, not for public works hut to meet
ordinary expenditure. And, though he only paid £43,000
Into the fund, and the interest on the £BOO,OOO or £900,000
which still remains to credit of that affair is not likely to
reach £40,000, lie borrows no less than £145,000. The
explanation given is that some amounts which might
have been borrowed in past years were not borrowed, hut
considering how carefully the fund was cleaned out each
year this seems highly improbable. The common theory
is this: The Govt, has lent money to local bodies, and
their sinking-funds, which are really trust money,
are in the Treasurer’s care. It is alleged that Mr.
Ward has commenced to borrow them, as well as the
State’s own sinking-fund, to square his shortage, and, if
so, Maoriland is surreptitiously using borrowed money for
ordinary revenue purposes. At all events, sundry ques-
tions asked in the House have been replied to with a great
scarcity of information, and Mr. Ward’s finance is begin-
ning to he viewed with some suspicion. Ward’s principal
critic, by the way, is Stout, whose own finance was the
most awful thing imaginable.
• • • •
According to the complete returns for the year
ended June’9s, the N.S.W. Post and Telegraph Depart-
ment shows a deficit of £123,332 on working expenses
only (if interest on money borrowed for construction pur-
poses is included it is about £lOO 000 more); Queensland
has a deficit of £81,390; Weslralia a deficit of £16,823,
and Tasmania is short by £17,048. On the other hand,
Victoria makes a profit of £73,167 ; South Australia
makes £17,683, and Maoriland makes £67,675. The work-
ing exp nses of the posts and telegraphs are, on the
whole, nearly half as much as those of the State railways,
hut the receipts are less than one-quarter as much. None
of the Australasian postal departments, except, perhaps,
those of Vic. and Maoriland, would pay their way if charged
w ith interest on cost of construction, and the majority
don’t even come within gunshot range of paying working
expenses alone. The postal and telegraph shortage, in
several cases, is very much worse than (he railway short-
age, and in proportion to the amount of capital invested
it is as much as 30 times worse; and the chief cause of
the trouble is political log-rolling. Vet, somehow, the
country, which worries so much about railway deficits,
never seems even to notice the postal deficit. Why not
apply the independent-commissioner system to the post
amt telegraph departin' nt ? It is as much needed there
as if ever was on the railways.

The affairs of the Equitable Life Assurance
Society ■ f t ie G.S. have been carefully overhauled by the
New York State auditor, and after a valuation by experts
of all ti e society’s securities, and a legal examination into
the titles of th" properties, and a careful audit of the
accounts, tills ind pendent authority reports that the
Equitable is in a thoroughly sound and prosperous condi-
tion. A State audit may not be an absolutely perfect
guarantee there is nothing in this world that is abso-
lutely perfect—but, at all events, it is the best one yet
invented, and the above report is something for the
Equitable to be proud of.

The peaceful, unobtrusive collapse of the N.S.
Wahs nuilding and investment institutions still goes on.
Last week’s record begins with the Australian Mutual
Investment and Building Co., which reconstructed in ’93,
and now proposes to re-reconstruct on the following
a l, - ' ei- sipairising basis: The last two years’ deposit
interest up to 28th July, ’95, is to be added to the prin-
a,,,.., ,id of being paid in cash. After that date
interest Is to cease altogether. When certain preferential
claims have been satisfied the depositors are to begin to
get their money back at the rate of 6d. in the £, when-
ever the co. is in a position to pay the 6d., hut the date
of repayment is wholly indefinite. Depositors who are in
a hurry may, however, take out 85 per cent, of their
claims in property, but whether the property is to be
taken at the co’s own valuation is not stated. As the
depositor, however, must either take the property or else
wait an indefinite number of years for Ills money without
interest, he probably will have to accept the co’s valua-
tion and he thankful. The A.M.I. and B. Co., according
to the latest available figures, owes in N.S.W. £247,000
to depositors and £llO,OOO on other liabilities, and its net
assets in the province are set down at £209,000, of
which £lO are in cash. This means that, the shortage is
£148,000. The last div. was declared in 1891.

The Southern Building Society of Goulburn
(N.S.W.) wants a three years’ extension of its deposits

» * » •
St. Joseph’s Investment and Building Society,
Sydney, a large concern of 27 years good standing, the
other day obtained a reduction of deposit-interest from
sto 3 per cent. A valuation of the society’s properties
by Richardson and Wrench now reveals the necessity for
writing off £47,301 10s. for further depreciation, and the
shares have therefore been reduced in value by £4 10s.
apiece. Some time ago £2 10s. per share was written off,
and the result is that the shares which, in 1892, were paid
up to £lO are now paid to £3 only, and nearly three-
fourths of the capital has entirely vanished. The society
has £lBO,OOO of deposits, and its whole assets are
£233,000. The net profit of the business for the year is
only £2500, and the beard reports that even the much-
reduced values placed on its properties are almost wholly
nominal, as sales are practically impossible. The selling-
value of an article which nobody wants to buy is always a
rather uncertain affair.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The Newcastle Permanent Investment and
Building Society reports that the profits of the year have
been more than absorbed by the insatiable item of depre-
ciation. Therefore there is now a debit balance at profit
and loss of £4406. Under these painful circumstances,
shareholders who have given notice of withdrawal are
requested, by way of a small, unofficial reconstruction, to
accept 4 per cent, interest instead of the 6 per cent, due
to them “in order to avoid an appeal to the Supreme
Court.” If they don’t accept, the society will presum-
ably appeal to the Court and reconstruct formally.

The Bulletin would much like to know, as a
matter of certainty, w hether, when a life-policy falls due,
either through death or maturity of the endowment, and
the amount is p rid over, the recipient will be liable, under
the N.S.W. law, to pay income tax upon it as part of the
year’s income. In Victoria an attempt has to
collect, tax in this f ishion, and in this paper’s opinion the
action is wholly indefensible. When a life-policy falls
due, it simply means that the savings of many years are
taken out in a lump from the place where they were
deposited, and the amount isn’t income, hut principal.
Moreover, in most eases, the insurance-policy only comes
in when the family breadwinner is dead, and if the widow
is charged prohate expehses and legacy duty and mis-
cellaneous fees, and 2J per cent, income-tax on all her
little pile, and then invests it and has also to pay income-
tax on the interest it yields, it, seems a case of stepping
pretty heavily on the bereaved family.

The Perpetual Executors and Trustees .Associa-
tion (Melii.) reports that it has made a loss of £197 as the
result of a rather desolate year’s operations. Also it has
been considered necessary to set aside £5900 of the reserve
to meet further possible josses, so that that fund is now
reduced to £lO,OOO ; and the co. lias a robust overdraft of
£3451. The earnings of the trustee business for ttie year
were only about £2OOO, and the working expenses and
rent were £3756, so that the interest earned by the invest-
ment of the co.’s own capital had to fill the gap. In other
words, it lived for the year mostly by chewing its own
tail. The trouble is said to be largely owing to the fact
that the co’s office property has been vacant for 18
months, a firm which formerly rented it having
ceased to do so; and as a new tenant has now been
found, the concern won’t live quite so much on its own
tail in future.

It is not only proposed in Melbourne to make
compulsory the registration of all assignments (with a
view to killing secret compositions), hut also to make the
records open to the public on payment of a fee.
The bill prepared provides that an assignment
must he assented to by a majority of creditors in number
and value, and further that no release shall be granted un-
less assented to by a four-fifths majority of creditors in
number and value. It further proposes to wipe out the
street corner assignee, and the assignee who ha : no “ re-
engnis d ” commercial standing. Recognised h.v whom ?
Whoever " whom ” may be, he or they take good care that
any estate worth having does not drift away from those
now in the swim.
The Vic. Trade Protection Societies will not
relish the proposed fee. They now have to pay 2s. 6d. for
every writ copied (and they must copy all of them), Is.
each for hills of sale, and so on for other searches; the
proposed additional charge will scare them badly. In
ordinary times it. costs each co. fully £lOOO per an. for
search fees alone, and should the Registrar charge 2s. 6d.
each for assignments, the self-appointed guardians of
commerce will sicken of the job.

Re the charges against the Melh. magistracy,
Lormer and his friends should investigate further and
ascertain how many “ pubs.” are being run in the names
of the wives and other relatives of certain J’s.P. This
alone might throw an at least indirect light upon the roll-

• « * «
The hauls made lately by certain Melb. leather-
dealers, who foresaw a rise and bought heavily, are small
compared with the prospective profits of certain wheat-
operators. Wheat now booms owing to the dismal pros-
pects of the coining harvest. Estimated balance of the
last two seasons’ wheat held by Melh. grain dealers on
Oct. 25, last, was 426,824 bags. As most of this is held
under lien by the grain co’s and the big operators who
bought at an average of, say, Is. lOd. a bushel, there is
quite a Coolgardie in store for the lucky holders; at time
of writing wheat was firm at 3s. 10d., and will probably
reach 4s. by the time this is in print.
• » • •
Twelve years ago Vic. experienced an all-round
increase in grain-values equalling the present rise. In
Melb. last week one big line of wheat changed hands four
times. One firm haa cleared £12,000 out of its wheat,
oats and barley deals.
• • • •
Latest move in the Manly ferry competition
a reduction of fares to Bd. for a 14-mile trip. But it can-
not last; something will have to burst. How the conser-
vative P.J.S.S. Co., compelled to initiate the new reduc-
tions (instantly followed by the Co-operative Co.), must
bemoan iis own hullheadness in times when clients
pleaded for an alteration of the old tariff 1
This competition looks likely t o end in the death
of one of the two cos. The origiinri P.J.S.S. Co. tariff was
ls. 6d., and, on its refusing to reduce to Is., the residents
organised a co-operative co. which did (he trip for 9d.
The old co. thereupon came down to 6d., and its rival
speedily did likewise. In last week’s papers it was
announced that the younger co. had contracted for the
building of a fine steamer, capable of doing the trip in
15min. less time than at present. A day or so after, the
old co. reduced its season-tickets 50 per cent. 1 The
transparency of the dodge was apparent. The Co-opera-
tive Co., which, so far, has been immensely successful,
readily accepted the challenge, and reduced its season-
tickets to the same level, and its daily fares from 6d.
to 3d. ! The battle will now be watched with much in-
terest. How the old co. can manage to carry on at this
ruinous tariff—considering its already deplorable financial
position—is something puzzling. Its shares, paid up to
£3, are saleable at Id.
• ♦ ♦ •
Westralia in 1893 received only 32,000 telegrams
during the whole year. In Aug. and Sept, of this year
W.A. got 23,000 per month. At present Adelaide is send-
ing on about 1100 per day.
The W.A. Post and Telegraph Department is
in a deplorable state of muddle. Recently 1200 telegrams
awaited despatch, and operators were working for two
days and nights on holidays to clear the line.
* ♦ ♦ *
A man who lately travelled 2400 sheep from the
head of the Australian Bight to Coolgardie (1200 miles)
says the telegraph-line on the W.A. side of Euola is in a
terrible state. Hundreds of poles are so rotten that he
could easily have kicked them over. He foreshadows a
serious block.
• * • *
People snigger at the gradations of salaam as
displayed by a well-known Melb. auctioneering firm in its
Argus sale-advts. In one case the auctioneers “ are in-
structed to sell.” in another they "have received in-
structions.” Further on they “ will sell.” But, in the
case of Mrs. Joseph Clarke, they have “ been honored
with instructions to sell.”
♦ » ♦ •
Re the seizure of £lOOO worth of contraband
jewellery at Sydney, an obviously-inspired S.M. Herald
paragraph says that: “As far as can tie learned it has not
yet been decided whether there shall be any prosecutions.
lt. is thought by some of the officers that the ends of
justice would he met by the confiscation of the goods.”
This is the old spare-the-poor-attorney argument in its
commercial form. One of the calamities of Australia is
the widespread doctrine that to swindle the State is no
crime. If anybody has swindled the Customs he should
go to gaol as does any other criminal (except a lawyer).
Apropos, twelve hundred (1200) imported
blankets were seized in one day recently by Vic. Customs,
on the ground that they were pine cotton made up to re-
present wool. They were marked “all cotton” as re-
quired by the Act, said mark being easily removeahle
with a damp sponge. And the Ms.l.. A. who foamed so
dreadfully about alleged local shoddy haven’t said a
word.
♦ * * •
An evil winked at by Tasmanian legislators is
the "truck” system in vogue at most of the southern
saw-mills. Onoe a man gets into the clutches of mill
employment (writes a Bulletin correspondent) he is
“ fixed.” Long hours at the hardest of rough bush work,
with the attendant miseries of mud and slush in winter—
and all this for little more than food and clothing: that
is what it means in the long run t The “ hands ” are for-
bidden to deal away from the mill. The crews of the
small vessels trading for timber, which are generally
owned by the mill proprietor, have strict orders not to
bring any uoods for the employes : violation of this rule
means "sack." Married men are the ones who feel it
worst, being powerless to move. These are the men who
are preferred and wanted, as, having families, they are
good consumers, and as they generally manage to get
into debt, they could not leave if they wanted to.

Will the roll of the unrolled stretch out to the crack of

moon,’’ to Ids servants, “ I’ll string ye up like onions, 50
on a rope.” Belter suspend the whole profession and un-
hang those who deserve it.

Williamstown (Vic.) mill is turning out 5000
yards tweed weekly, and has hooked forward orders to
keep it going at hreak-neck speed well into the autumn
season. Ballarat mill prospers, having made £2699 last
half-year, and pays 3d. div. on pref. shares. The busy
aspect of the provincial Vic. woollen mills is rather a
facer tor Assembly men who raised the " shoddy ” cry
to damage native production. The public now more
freely buy Vic. goods, satisfied that the revelations
were more injurious to the imported article.

claim at Wyalong. The reef, which measures from 12m.

Theodore Durrant, organist of the Em-
manuel Baptist church, San Francisco, has been
convicted of outraging and murdering Blanche
Lament, a co-teacher with him in the Sunday-
school, whose dead body was found last Easter
Sunday wedged up in the belfry. Another girl
nam'd Minnie Williams, a member of the Sunday-
school Bible class, also fell a victim to Durrant,
and bet body was found in the library of the
church on Easter Saturday. The proceedings
in connection with the trial of Durrant,
against whom a - strong primd facie case ex-
isted, have extended over seven months—a
striking testimony to the tardiness of San
Francisco justice. Yet even this delay is prefer-
able to the system which, in some of the Austra-
lian provinces, prejudges in haste, and repents—
when it is compelled.
# # #
A Cap-day Episode,
A well-known juvenile plunger, attired in latest
London style, did Derby-day with bad results to
his pocket; winding up by planking his all on the
last race, and going home stone broke. However,
he easily raised some money from his friends, and
shone again in all his glory on Cup-day. At
first, however, the fates were again against him ;
the Cup was coming on, he felt certain he knew
the winner, but could not raise another pound on
the course. Waiting an opportune moment, he
made his way to the bookmaker with whom he
had been betting and said, “Look here, Alf., old
man, I’m broke and have the winner of the Cup,
lend me ss. on this cigar-case, it is filled with
B. L. Corto Rothschilds cigars, and they cost me
6d. each.” “B. L. Corto Rothschilds,” said the
book, “ give me one —quick. Will I lend you 55.,
will I bless you all my life? What’s your fancy?”
The youth told him. “ .£25 to nothing, that is
what I’ll lay you—keep your case, but if you win
you must band me a box of 25 of those cigars,
they are the best in the world.” Our friend’s
fancy won and he got his £25, and before the day
was over he had won £lOOO. The B. L. Corto
Rothschilds are acknowledged by all keen judges
to be the beat value in the world at fid. each.*

WOLFE’S
SCHNAPPS.
ABSOLUTELY PURE.
Society
N.S.W. Council, when discussing the in-
come-tax exemption, tried to define a “ living
wage,” and one fatted nominee named Cox
rendered himself famous by announcing that “ a
man and his wife and five children could live on
I4s. a week ; he regarded 14s. a week as a living
wage for a man and his family.” The Bulletin
respectfully asks Mr. Cox if he will devote, say,
three months of next Parliamentary recess to an
attempt to live with a wife and five children (no
restrictions made as to whose wife she is), and buy
clothes for himself and for them, boots, hats, coal,
firewood, food, keep the furniture in repair, pay
for rent, school fees, lighting, medicine if need be,
and supply all other wants on 20s. a week, let
alone 145., and yet not degenerate into a gaunt,
shoeless, long-haired, matted, wolfish spectre and
a barbarian. And if he won’t make the attempt
then he should for ever hold his peace as a man
who hasn’t the courage of his own bad opinions.
• • «
Billy White laments the march of the
New Woman. “ Billy,” chief of the remnant of
a Westralian tribe, lately took to wife one Willa-
gin, a half-caste, and to celebrate, they made a
bee-line to the nearest Settlers’ Home. Police-
man X. happened to be there, and the landlord
was wary ; so Willagin caroused “on her own.”
Westralian law allows a “ dhrop” to a half-blood,
but denies the luxury to the bona-fide “nig.”
Willagin disported herself in the bar; Billy
gazed wistfully in, and when the policeman
appears, says Billy : “Look here, why you no let
me get rum ? You let my woman—all my money.”
X. is conciliatory and explains. But Billy doesn’t
see the logic of it, and with voice rising rapidly :
“All plurry well, she get grog, me stay outside—
and I'm de boss ! ” Just put all the sarcasm into
that that Billy did, if you want the real flavor.

Photo. by Brand.
Little IVY SCOTT, the Sydney child vocalist, of “Off to the Rio
Grande” celebrity.
A week or two back Melb. Block was
startled by the spectacle of a knickerbockered
damsel swaggering along Swanston-street. Two
or three hundred astounded males gathered to
watch developments, but when the lady stepped
daintily on to the front seat of a South Yarra
tram and extended her shapely legs before her,
even the ranks of Tuscany could not forbear to
cheer, and the tram disappeared southward with
a mighty hooray in its wake. In certain papers
reference was made to the incident, the social
status of the lady being spoken of in most dis-
paraging terms. As a matter of fact, the fair one
is a leading sassiety cyclist who had met with an
accident to her “jigger,’’ and was compelled to
return home without it. Since the publicity, the
lady’s hubby has, it is said, shut down sternly on
bikes and knickerbockers.
# * *
The dramatic sequel to the nuptials of that
gilded Melb. youth and his blushing bride, erst-
while the shapely seat-warmer at a local music-
hall show, is that both the parties were, imme-
diately after the happy event, taken under the
wing of the officiating clergymaff; and after resid-
ing at his parsonage for a few weeks they decided
to cast off forever the tights and neckties sym-
bolical of the world, the flesh, and the devil, and to
embrace the mother church rather than the nearest
barmaid or chappie. The conversion was soon
completed, and to-day the happy couple, ecstatic
in their new-found faith, are skimming over the
China Sea en route for the Flowery land, where
they intend starting mission work. It makes one
smile!
* m *
It is coming ! The other day the Anglican
Bishop and the Cathedral precentor (the latter pro-
secutor in a case of disturbing a religious service)
were accommodated with seats on the bench at a
Sydney police-court. The presiding stipendiary
(Oapt. Fisher) is, by the way, the son of an arch-
deacon (of Calcutta). As all are supposedly equal
before the law, no party to a suit, or anyone con-
nected with him, should be on the judgment seat.
The administration of alleged justice in N.S.W.
has fallen into such inexpressible contempt in con-
nection with several recent celebrated cases that
even the smallest aggression on the impartiality
which should characterise the proceedings of the
courts is worth nothing.
* • »
At Sydney Central court one day last week
a prisoner complained of the number of aliases
placed opposite his real name on the charge-sheet.
The stipendiary overruled the objection, where-
upon the man behind the spikes objected to the
arresting detective remaining in court and exam-
ining the witnesses. He was again overruled, the
magistrate adding, “You seem well up in police-
court practice.” “I am sorry I cannot return
the compliment,” was the retort. Both the
accused man’s complaints were perfectly legiti-
mate. Placing a number of aliases on the charge-
sheet is only a silent and venomous method of in-
forming the bench that the accused is “known.”
Also, the arresting constable should be outside
with the other witnesses. The police are not
such angels that their testimony should be re-
garded as intrinsically better than that of ordinary
citizens.

The wood-work of
the prison buildings at
Port Arthur (now called
Carnarvon), Tas., is
slowly but surely being
demolished by time and
English ivy. The latter
will soon hide even the
bloodstained stones of
the. buildings. Massive
cell doors, unopened for
a quarter of a century,
have been forced from
their position by the
ever-strengthening ten-
drils of the plant. The
rusted iron bars travers-
ing the glassless win-
dows have been thrust
aside by the insinuating
arms of the ivy, and the
vigorous parent stem
outside in the sunlight
is continually pouring
in an army of yellow,
sickly - looking feelers
which steal like serpents
across the rotting floors,
through the damp and
gloomy cells, and up and
down the dark stair-
cases. Truly, all nature
is conspiring to obliter-
ate this horrible monu-
ment of man’s inhu-
manity to man. The
persistency of the ivy
deserves every encour-
agement.
Gippsland possesses the largest earth-worm
in the world — Megascolides Australis. Ordinary
specimens measure between four and five feet long
and one inch thick, but Prof. M‘Coy once
measured a living one, six feet long. It lives
principally in sloping creek banks, but is some-
times found under logs. When it hears a noise
on the surface above its burrow it retreats
rapidly from the sound, making a distinct
gurgling sound as it does so. Alive, it has a
curious smell, much like that of creosote ; dead,
the odour is worse than that of the strongest and
smellfullest creosote. The dead body by decay
passes into an oily fluid, which Gippslander
assert is very good for rheumatism. Fowls refus 8
to touch the worm, either living or dead. Hel e
d
in the hand the worm contracts its body, and,
from a row of dorsal pores extending almost the
whole length of the back, squirts out jets of milky
fluid to a height of several inches. Other very
big worms have been found in India, Ceylon and
South Africa, but up to date Megascolides is the
daddy of the lot.
It is quite evident that a lot of these worms
journeyed to Melbourne to welcome Gov. Brassey.
PRAYING FOR RAIN.
“At Hannan’s water is very scarce, quantities being brought in from
White Feather. Frequently the hotels can supply nothing but raw
whisky, not even beer, and no tea is provided at meals. People are
rushing into Coolgardie, where water is plentiful. The residents of
Hannan’s are execrating the Government and praying for a big thunder-
storm.”-WESTHALLAN TELEGRAM.
“ I WOULD be thankful for a little rain; still I CAN take it neat."
The Bulletin could smell them at Sydney, 600
miles away, in a south wind.

One fat society lady, after publicly describ-
ing a series of beautiful meandering curves, has
discovered that the admixture of cycling with
whisky is incompatible with safety.
A middle-aged Tas-
manian Adonis of gigan-
tic proportions was lately
induced by some lady
friends to go to a fancy-
dress ball as Rob Roy
Macgregor—or some such
fiend. The whole after-
noon before the festival
he sat in his garden
with his knees bared to
a furious sun until he
reckoned they were done
to a turn. Then he
donned the costume and
walked forth to the
gathering of the clans.
During the Highland
fling Macgregor’s brawny
knees began to crack and
presently flaked off. The
whirlwinds emanating
from the ladies’ dresses
playfully caught up the
fragments of Rob, and
soon the ball-room was
like a garden when the
dead leaves fall. Square
inches of Scotchman
floated gently on to the
dancers and even pene-
trated into the supper-
room, and the air at
length became so charged
with bits of Rob Roy
Macgregor that what was
left of him was induced
to quit with a fringe of
burnt pelt hanging over
earh stocking and his
knees glowing like a
couple of head • lights.
He went to bed and
stayed there for a week
with an oil poultice on
each knee.

Adelaide undertakers
assert that Anglican and
R.O. clergymen always
charge fees for burials,
and that parsons of other
denominations act gra-
tuitously except for
“ deaders ” outside their
own flock, or when the
undertaker tries to make
a convenience of them.
Apropos of the sweating of hospital nurses
in Melbourne. The Wellington (M.L.) hospital
nurses now work on the eight-hours system.
Result, improvement both in health and in
efficiency.
W ellingfcon
(M.L.) is mainly a
wood - built city,
and if you live in a
terrace you can
hear, through the
thin timber parti-
tion, the remarks of
your next door
neighbor quite
clearly. Strangers,
especially the Eng-
lish variety, don’t
understand this
local peculiarity at
first. Recently some
English people took
a house in a Wel-
lington terrace and
lived there for one
calendar week.
Then they explained
to the landlord that
they must really
leave unless the
man next door left
olf pounding his
brawny cheat while
he was having his
bath in the morn-
ing. The landlord
replied that the
man next door was
the only reliable
pay in the terrace,
and that a tenant
such as he was
might slap his chest
or any other part
of himself as loudly
as he liked at any
period of the day
or night. So the
English family left
in disgust.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback-the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex_
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle ioby s Rolled Oats.

An address of welcome to a new Governor
from the inmates of a local Benevolent Asylum is
no laughing matter. It’s a BlK'ekmg mstance of
the humorless spirit in which loyalty is
worked.
• • •
The popular Tory belief that every work-
ing man is a mannerless pig, who “scoffs ’ his
victuals with weird noises and casts the bones to
his wife, is not likely to be dispelled by the be-
havior of certain Vic. Labor-members at public
functions.
# * •
Strictly untruthful accounts of the Brassey
"mention were published by Melbourne’s rival
-veiling journals. Each narrative, being built
'non the official programme, gave out that Brenner
Turner and Attorney-General Isaacs rode into
town in the same carriage with the new Governor
and his wife, whereas these two statesmen sat
visibly depressed (Isaacs was, anyway) in a pre-
ceding carriage. Wallington and another richly-
caparisoned person enjoyed the glory which was
to have been theirs. According to the Queen s
regulations, or Imperial etiquette, a Governor on
the supreme occasion of being sworn-m must be
“ attended ’’ by the two people who sit with their
backs to the horses. Ministers of the Grown
would rank as companions, not ‘attendants
and half of the public hysteria would be absorbed
by the minor planets, so to speak, though the
multitude might really be intending it all for his
Excellency.
Therefore, Turner and Isaacs, just as they
began to prepare for facing the new idols en route,
were tapped on the shoulder by Wallington and
told to regard their dream as over, and their hopes
as fled. The extra-speshal organs of evening
opinion committed another brazen tarradiddle in
stating that Brassey hung-fire upon bt. Kilda
pier whilst the “ fresh voices of 2000 children rang
out the National Anthem.” Nothing of the kind
happened. The attempt to give Brassey a musical
treat was made before he arrived at the pier.
The melody was to have come floating o er tne
waters, but merciful breezes blew the noise back
into the yawning chasms of those 2000 children,
and Brassey escaped their artless twang, it is
only fair to admit that the youngsters might have
made a much worse noise than they did. For 1 1
most part they merely opened tneir monotonous
countenances and grinned at their own loyal
absurdity.

Three days after arrival Lady Brassey
went shopping in Democratic Melbourne. A
gaping and increasing crowd followed m her
wake up and down two streets. Finally they
“treed ” her in a drapery establishment, and the
mob blocked the footpath. The proprietors slid
Her “ Lydyship” out through the back-door into
Little Bourke-street, and she sneaked home
through the rotten-cabbage ways of Ghowlancl.
# * *
There is already grave discord at Melb.
Govt, House. All through the Hopetoun i regime,
the man who had the management of the show
business, and was even most conspicuous on
“State” occasions, was private-sec. Wallington.
He was Hopetoun’s guide, philosopher and friend,
and no doubt thought he was going to be the
same under Brassey, judging from the proceedings
at the first lev6e. But that assertive young man,
the Earl of Shaftesbury, arose in his wrath, and
snorted rebellious blasts, and at the next levee he
played first fiddle, Wallington being relegated
to his propah position.
# # #
The Brasseys have been “kodaked” all
over Melbourne, Men and women armed he in
wait for Lady B. as she goes shopping, and directly
she steps in or out of her carriage (lifting her skirts
in the act) there is a loud click, and half-a-dozen
.ictures of her ankle are registered. Directly Gov.
Brassey stepped ashore at St. Kilda, ‘ click, click,
click,” went the instruments as he kissed his
daughter, Mrs. Freeman-Thomas.

S.A. Gov. Buxton’s reception was a very
slow affair, and the cheers and enthusiasm alleged
by the press were mostly imaginary. The
Governor is a much older man than would appear
by his portraits, but there is no mistaking his
evangelical face. He made his best impression as
a speaker, in which particular he_ is far above
Kintore. As an orator, he is as deliberate and as
impressive as a temperance lecturer, but he calls
Governor “ Governah ” and speaker “ speakah.
H.E. also resembles the average lecturer in wait-
ing at the impressive points for applause.
There was an element of cheapness about the
reception. The Protector, the S.A. warship, was
manned bv a volunteer crew, and fired a salute
out of obsolete guns. The police escort consisted
of men who should have been off duty, and who
received no extra pay for extra work ; the militia
received a reduced allowance for the day s duty ;
the civil servants were in blazing uniforms, but
mostly borrowed or hired, and even H.E. himself
had had his screw cut down the previous week to
.£4OOO p.a. Which accounts for the lack of
enthusiasm in the proceedings.

My Dear Moorabinda —
A notable Cup festival was the charity ball in
aid of the Melbourne district nursing society,
held in Melb. Town Hall last Thursday—notable
principally because it introduced the Brasseys in
a terpsichorean sense. The new Governor, in
evening kit, looked like Ryley’s Duke “In Town,”
seen sideways in a soup-ladle ; but I didn’t like
his way of wearing his order—like a necktie with
the bauble hanging d la woman’s locket from
beneath the conventional white dress-necktie-bow.
It was a gory ribbon, too, and suggested that the
brand-new Gov. had cut his throat in despair
before starting. From all exterior signs it looks
as if Gov. Brassey would be horribly popular—for
one thing, his way of dancing is so hearty. No
tips-of-fingers.turning for his Victorian Ex., who
grabbed his partner warmly round thewaist, though
she was a splinter-shaped matron of not more than
the average official beauty, and the dance was an
otherwise frigid vice-regal set. Also, Gov. B. is
of the exuberant school of dancing which marks
the time with a stamp and advances to meet a
partner head-first, and this sort of thing goes, you
know, in a land of barn-dancers.
I thought Lady Brassey looked very well in
grey and sunbeam-yellow brocade with yellow
crSpe sleeves and a large yellow feather fan—for,
of course, she couldn’t help the committee bouquet
no!"harmonising, and she wore most of her dia-
monds on her head. Lady B. is a waltzing gover-
noress, and “warmed the floor ” for the fifth
waltz by taking a few lightning twists with the
ugliest aide, who was, of course, an earl. You
can generally spot Vere de Vere in these degene-
rate days by his striking resemblance to a death’s-
head. The aides are not a fatally fascinating
bevy. One looks like a schoolboy—another has
ears like fans and the facial expression of a puzzled
flying-fox, while yet another could always make a
living figuring as a fearful result of not taking so-
and-so’s elixir.
Lady Clarke adorned the long-drawn out dais
at the ball which I believe I am talking about,
and was apparently in the seventh heaven be-
cause her combination of colors was the same as
Lady Brassey’s. Another amusing thing was the
spectacle of a girl ingenuously pinning down a
naval man’s necktie in the middle of the ballroom.
A sprinkling of Sydneyites turned up, including a
Sydney beauty in white and pink, who seemed to
be suffering from an unusual rush of blank spaces
to her dance-card ; but most of the Sydney con-
tingent stood around on one foot, and appeared to
hang fire from a terpsichorean point of view,
,hougb, as I saw men promenading with a girl on
( ach arm, perhaps a plus of petticoats had some-
thing to do with it.
Derby Day at Flemington made a good drapery
* how, taking it by and large—not that everybody
h ad her Sunday frock on, for there were lots of
weet simplicity in cambric—a material which is
s out of place on a racecourse as it would be in a
all-room. One fair damsel in a blush-pink cotton
was pretty enough to carry it off, but others ought
to have remembered that only beauty is privileged
to go unadorned and still make a splash. A
homely woman never looks so hopeless as she does
in white muslin with a red rose in her bowsprit.
The vice-regal box showed plenty of primal color.
Lady Brassey (whose portraits are too young for
her—she has two Kyrle-Bellewish intervals of grey
in her hair, just over each ear) presented rather a
tuppence-colored effect in royal purple silk, with
cheese-color lace collars, a toque with crimson
roses in it, a white fall with a black spot, and a
cheese-color umbrella. The flowers presented to
her en route by a loyal mayoralty were left in the
carriage, by the way—which quite supports the
idea that you can tell what a woman thinks of
you by the way she treats the flowers you give her.
The vice regal box was not over-crowded, Mrs.
Freeman Thomas wore mauve and white, with a
hat mercifully matching; Lady Maud Warrender
(who appears to be a sort of cultivated Vital
Spark) wore pale-grey silk with pink flowers on
top, giving something the effect of a rosy cockatoo,
and another denizen of the V.R. enclosure wore
widie black and white stripes d la zebra. The
V.R. youths were not desperately effective, I
thought their race garments showed signs of hav-
ing been not wanted on the voyage, and so had
found time to settle into other than fashionable
creases. Even Gov. Brassey himself, with a
white boutonniere, was hardly up-to-date about
the hat-brim. And, apropos of the Governor’s
box, I really was surprised to see Rear-Admiral
Cyprian Bridge sunning himself therein. Despite
good resolutions, the navy can no more keep away
from Flemington in November than a oat can
keep away from cream.
Lady Holled Smith wore black satin, with a
wall-paper pattern in strawberry pink, and
trimmed with butter-color lace—a crushed-straw-
berry hat trimmed with black and white chiffon
and a butter-color aigrette. Orepon was very
popular—Mrs. Edward Millar wore a lemon-color
gown made of wool orepon ; Mrs. Bowes Kelly a
black cr4pon skirt with a pink shot-silk blouse.
The blouse effect had many followers at the
Derby, though it never presents a full-dress
appearance—there is always more or less of a
“ reach -me - down ” air about it, I think.
But, judging from the number of smart people
who wore it at Flemington, we are in for a skirt-
and-blouse summer. Mrs. Percy Chirnside wore
an artistically-blended garment in fawn-color and
pale-blue, and Miss Mary Clarke’s white satin,
sprinkled with roses, was like Dresden china.
Decima Moore, squired by Cecil Hope, turned
up in black and silver and a Hamlet hat; Miss
Elsie Cameron (Mrs. J. R. Cowell) wore a bluey-
green checked silk blouse over a peacock-blue
erepon skirt.
The Sydney contingent was not exceedingly
spruce. Miss Nellie Hill wore dark-blue with a
white bird’s-eye dot, white chiffon front and white
gloves to the elbow —hat in yellow and black ;
Miss Florrie Hill a dark skirt and a grey-and-
black check silk blouse with a splotch of coxcomb-
red in her hat; another Sydney spinster wore a
dismal snuff-color suit. Also, a Jersey-haired
Sydneysider unwisely wore green with a crimson
pattern like a fire in a grass-paddock ; but Mrs.
W. B. Mitchell’s Derby dress suggested dewy
cobwebs or melted pearls. Miss Macarthur also
wore grey softened with white chiffon which be-
came her to aT. There was no particular color
prominent at the Derby.
I cannot say I approved in toto of the Derby
man, from a modish standpoint; taking the aver-
age, there was too much straw-hat and sac-coat
visible. And while Guvmentouse is a power in
the land, a chappie who hasn’t a coat to his heels,
a curly brim to his hat, and a boutonniere like a
catherine-wheel, cannot be considered a chappie,
don’tcherknow. But men are becoming such rare
birds nowadays, that they are getting careless
how they dress. Women preponderated in the
Derby crowd to an amazing extent—which is one
of the worst results of the male exodus to Gool-
gardie, I suppose. No wonder Melbourne women
are bringing breeohes-of-promise actions against
the executors of their dead deceivers.

J. Howe, who shore 321 sheep in Bhrs.

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.
Better than Gold.
There are but few things in this world more
highly appreciated or eagerly sought after than
gold. Why? Not because it is an attractive
color; not because it weighs more than any other
metal; not because it lengthens life. No. But
it brings the necessaries, and, if plentiful, the
luxuries of life. It gives a certain social standing
to its possessor; it brings friends, flattery, and
smiles, and veneers ungentlemanly, or even other-
wise questionable actions, and smooths pathways
otherwise rough. But when the dear wife, or son,
or daughter lies stricken with pain and feebleness;
when disease racks the mind and body; when the
dear one loses that natural buoyancy, sparkle,
energy, and vitality ; then the glitter of gold
becomes almost repulsive ; its uselessness brings a
loathing. Mr. W. R. Lawson, of the firm of
Lawson & Grieve, the well-known shoeing forge,
Oamperdowu, Viet., says : “ While suffering from
constantly recurring attacks of lumbago, which
were little short of continuous, I tried all sorts of
remedies without effect, including injections of
morphia, which only acted temporarily in allaying
pain but produced no permanent improvement,
and my medical was incapable of relieving
me. I then commenced Warner’s Safe Cure, and
although it may seem strange I actually felt relief
from the first dose 1 took, and when I had finished
taking one bottle I was all right again. I feel
much pleasure in stating that I attribute my re-
covery to this medicine alone.” To him, as to
thousands of others, it hasproven better than gold.*
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Trier came as a boon and a blessing to the
main body of N.S. W. punters last Saturday. They all
backed Messrs. Brennan and Noake’s horse for the Hotham
Handicap, whereas few of them won over Hindoo for the
Coburg Stakes. The runner-up, Reconstruction, was the
popular Sydney fancy that time.
Anyone judging the Derby horses by their looks
alone would have picked Osculator, Auraria, and Wallace
to beat Waterfall and the four rank outsiders of the
crowd. Certainly Osculator has an ugly lump, said to be
“callus,” on one of his knees, but otherwise the big
colt from Maoriland is as handsome as they breed ’em.
Fine and large proportions, nice head, and rich,
dark bay coat with four black points. A show
animal spoilt by the knee blemish aforesaid. Aura-
ria is rather more taking in appearance than Wallace,
and perhaps lime may show that the Derby race
was not a fair test of the relative merils of the three in a
li mile struggle. The Derby was distinctly slow for the
first three furlongs, and very fast for the remaining nine.
Waterfall seemed outclassed. The meanest - looking
animal in the field, he finished at the tail-end. Yet this
same Waterfall, with a light weight up, had run the same
distance at Caulfield in lOsec. less time, only a fortnight
earlier. So much for time trials, public or private.
Hova won the Melbourne Stakes in remarkably
easy style. A furlong from home jockey Jimmy Hayes
was holding his mount back and glancing from right to
left of him as though cocksure of being able to “ leave
ttie others standing still” at any moment. When Hova
got the hint to clear right away from the field the race
was ail over. Nevertheless, Music’s manner of running
second suggested that he might have a lot to do with the
finish for the Cup. Ruenalf, last year, beat Patron over
the Stakes mile and a quarter almost as easily as Hova
took them down on Saturday.
Mr. Sam Cook’s horses appear to be under a cloud
again. The Heroine is still lame from a mishap in the Caul-
field Cup. The Officer made a very poor show for the Mari-
byrnong, and perhaps is not nearly the colt they thought
him. At Caulfield there were two lots of two-year-old
“form.” The Officer, Thunder Queen and Norroy
finished in front for the Debutant Stakes (four furlongs),
whilst Newhaven, Coil and Kobold filled places in the
Nursery Handicap (4J furlongs). When it came to the
Maribyrnong sprint of five furlongs. Newhaven, Coil
and Kobold ran as before in a field of seven, Officer,
Norroy, and Thunder Queen being beaten out of places.
Ariki, the seventh straggler, was unplaced in each of the
three races, and so helped to make all the running look
genuine.
Whatever the real quality of Wallace as a racer,
there is no getting away from the fact that Mr. Wilson’s
chestnut ’orse had a great reputation before it was earned.
Sentiment and the gags of sporting journalism shortened
Wallace’s price for the Derby. He was “ the illustrious
son of Carbine,” with “ his sire’s disinclination to canter
to the starling-post,” and an alleged tendency to get
“ more like his dad every day,” and so forth. Also, the
touts swore that Wallace has “ the machine-like action of
his great progenitor,” which he certainly hasn't. The
sensational lottery colt is a true descendant of Musket in
the matter of sluggishness, but, when roused, he doesn’t
gallop with the wonderful Carbine and Carnage stride.
Carnage, at his best, was a far more attractive mover than
Wallace.
Lady Brassey “ did ” the saddling-paddook
with enthusiasm on Derby day. Governor B. showed no
special interest in horseflesh, but his spouse gazed at the
favorites through her double eyeglasses as though she
were trying to form a fancy on her own. The suite
johnnies in attendance also seemed to be in a turfy frame
of mind. One of them stroked Auraria’s nose with all
the confidence of a new-chum who doesn’t know that the
racehorses of this unculohawed country have a dislike to
kid gloves, as a rule, and a natural disposition to bite any
strange hand that wears one. Luckily, Auraria is the
most amiable filly 'going, and a puffed lydy. She
wouldn’t hurt even an aide-de-camp.
The new rule at Flemington of bringing the
Derby and Cup winners down the quiet course, instead of
among the Lawn mob, to be blue-ribboned, is an improve-
ment in every way. It gives the Governoress a chance to
accomplish her fell purpose without getting very hot over
it, and provides an amusing little incident for the people
on the Hill and Flat, who, of yore, couldn’t catch a
glimpse of the ceremony. Wallace and Lady Brassey
tickled the Hill’s fancy on Saturday. Each unsuccessful
attempt to lassoo the noble steed with his badge of total
abstinence fetched a round of applause. The horse played
to the gallery, and the lady was a novice at the business.
Besides, the effect of the bright blue ribbon against her
violet costume was not soothing. At last the badge got
there, assisted by Gov. Brassey, and the multitude let off
a cheer which signified “ encore.”
The Brassey triumphal barouche and fittings are
preferred by Smellbourne’s bong-tong to the late Hope-
toun equipage. Hopeloun used to cut a dash in Lord
Mayor style, with his heavy blue-and-yellow chariot and
flunkeys to match. Gov. B. goes in for the subdued
splendour of a ohocolate-and-white turn-out, which har-
monises with the buff-and-brown of the Mounted Rifles,
and melts into the sun-baked sward, so to speak. A
Governor can’t be too careful in such matters. Judging
from several remarks passed on Saturday, The Bulletin
would say that the Brassey kerridge will inspire a lot of
ardent loyalty to the British Crown.
This is perhaps the first Cup-time within the
memory of Melbourne’s oldest snob that the vice-regal
box at Flemington has been free from visiting Governors.
The writer has a fevered recollection of five ruling per-
sonages sitting in that same pen, all in a row. It was a
most brilliant spectacle, but perhaps somewhat too
exciting.

Melbourne is a city flowing with beer, tucker,
tobacco, doubles, and dead-beats:— (a) “ For Gourmands:
Half-pint beer, plate of hot ourry-and-rice, and sausage-
roll, 2d.” (6) “For Sportsmen: Beer or rum, cigar,
counter-lunch, and pick your own double (£l5 to
nothing), 3d.” The above is the programme at several
reputable pubs.
• • • *
A day or two after his arrival, Gov. Brassey,
accompanied by several ladies and aides, went for a row
on the Ynrra in the sumptuous pleasure-boat he brought
with him in the Sunbeam. Just opposite Brander’s Ferry
a four-oared club race was coming down, and as the north
side boat drew opposite the vice-regal party, bow had his
oar knocked violently out of his hand. At that moment
there arose in the wake of the racing boat and a few feet
from the stern of the Sunbeamers’ skiff, a corpse which
rode upright in the water as if anchored to the bottom,
and which swayed to and fro, its head bowing homage to
the new Governor. But the incident had no novelty.
Gov. Brassey had already been welcomed by the stiff.
* • • •
As all-round sports Gov. Brassey and his wife
promise to eclipse anything yet seen in the vice-regal
line. In the first week of their Melb. residence they have
been seen yachting, hunting, cycling and rowing.
• • * •
Writes “Scrip” Wallace’s great battle with
the Maorilander, Osculator, brought to mind his sire’s
nip-and-tuck tussle with Ensign, seven years ago, in the
same race, when a short head won the money, as on
Saturday. The youngster had better luck than his dad,
but he had to stretch his neck to some purpose. It was a
slow-run race in the early stages, just as Ensign’s was,
but the last six furlongs were skipped over in 1.16 and a
fraction. Wallace, although exhibiting many points in
the matter of quality, from a horsey man’s point of view,
looks small and mean-looking when compared with the
majestic Maorilander.
Osculator belongs to a M.L. bookie, and is yet a
maiden, with two places to his credit. Distance is his
forte —he never gets going until after a mile or so.
It was a slow-run Derby, the time, 2.46, being
7seo. behind the record held jointly by Trident and
Carnage.
The first three in “ the ribbon ” are by sons of
Musket—Carbine, Hotchkiss, and Trenton. Hotchkiss
never raced, but is beginning to make a name for himself
at the stud.
The Derby contest was essentially interpro-
vincial in character. The trio in the honors list, after the
official placing, represented Victoria, M.L., and S.A.
N.S.W. was out of it.
The erst owners of Wallace get .£5OO out of the
Derby Stake, besides the £2500 paid them by Mr. Wilson
for re-possession.
Coil was unable to bring Maribyrnong Plate
stake to N.S. W., as Arihi did last year. Newhaven, the
winner, is a taking colt, with credentials for classic con-
tests next year. The Officer found his added burden too
cumbersome. Wallace ran third in this event last season.
As the Derby field was pulling up it was dis-
covered that the first letters in the names of the placed
three spelt Woa.
Hova’s brilliancy is indisputable. He is un-
doubtedly the best pacer in the colonies, and, Carbine-
line. weight or distance doesn’t trouble him. He won
Melb. Stakes in an ordinary garden canter, smothering
his field most ridiculously. Hova’s sire, Ingomar, was
imported to M.L. from Ireland, where he was a champion
under colors. Had he lived, and been afforded the same
opportunities, there was every promise of his proving a
second Musket at the stud.
The cracking-up tactics adopted by the rider of
Hopscotch at Caulfield were again resorted to in Melb.
Stakes, with similar result. It was thought that a-quarter
mile shorter course would favor him in the pace-making
racket, and enable him to win from start to post. Field-
leading, with the object of galloping the rest to a stand-
still, isn’t always the policy it's cracked up to be, even in
the case of a good horse. Hova’s jock wasn’t frightened
of getting blocked in. He rode with the rest, and kept
his eye skinned.
Osculator carried more M.L. money in the
Derby than Carbine did when he contested the race.
Both had bad luck, heads deciding the stake against
them.
Maribyrnong Plate field this year was excep-
tionally small, only seven running. There were 30 in ’Bl,
when Segeuhoe won. As a rule, there are about a dozen
starters, but in ’7l, when the event was inaugurated, only
four paid up.
The erst owner of St. Alban’s only raffled his
equine string to buy most of them back again. He bought
six at the sale, including Challenger (£500) and Carbine’s
dam, Mersey (£300). C. M. Lloyd, understood to be also
buying for Wilson, secured six lots, including Havoc
(±1650) and Aura (dam of Auraria). Trainer M'Dnnald
was also a buyer. The surprise of the sale was Strath-
more going at 300 guineas. Carnage’s owner expected
over a thousand instead of half that amount. Mr. Wilson
will still race and breed, and Wallace has set things going
for him first time of asking.
The new owners of St. Alban’s don’t quite know
what to do with it. They will probably sell—if anyone
can be found in Melbourne with enough cash to buy.
Honda is about the unluekiest horse in Aus-
tralia. Though he got second in a Melb. Cup he is still a
maiden, and on Saturday he fell in the bigrace, and broke
his jockey’s arm.
* * ♦ »
Auraria, which The Bulletin tipped to win
both Derby and Cup, so far lived up to this paper’s faith
in her that she ran third in the Derby behind Wallace
and Osculator, and won the Melbourne Cup in good
style, with Hova second, an obscure animal named
Burrabari third, and Waterfall fourth. Auraria is
only the second mare to win the Melbourne Cup,
Briseis, in 1876, being the other one. This means only
two wins by mares in 36 years. Auraria is a three-year-
old daughter of Trenton-Aura, and she comes from South
Australia, which is morally rough on that evangelical
province and its evangelical Gov. In Auraria’s win may
be seen the linger of the Devil—luring Fowell-Buxton on
to the Turf.

in the St. Alban’s lottery was not one of the very lucky
fellows. Eureka, under the hammer, realised 16 guinea?
only.

The fame of owning a share in Wallace didn’t
stay long with the members of the lucky syndicate,
Photos, of Messrs. House and English were supplied to

back from them. The two up-country sports made s

* • * •
Chief Secretary Peacock was called in to wit-
ness the transfer of Wallace by the syndicate to Wilson.
Peacock is the member for their district, but he takes so
little interest in horse-racing that the momentous tran-
saction had to be explained to him. As official head of
the Vic. police dept, he was somewhat shocked to hear
that a racehorse had been publicly svveeped.
♦ * ♦ v
Mr. Arundell, who won Trenton in the St.
Alban’s lottery, was formerly clerk to Messrs. Davies and
Campbell, of Melbourne, solicitors to the Modern Building
Society. The society is a creditor of Mr. Arundell, and
has injuncted Mr. W. R. Wilson not to deliver the horse.
However, Mr. ArundelTs creditors have agreed to respect
his wife's half-share in the lottery-ticket, and all will end
harmoniously.
* * * *
The head teachers of Prahran (Vic.) asked the
local Board of Advice to make Cup-day a school-holiday.
The Board did so without a murmur. This action would
not have been allowed to pass without opposition from
the pious party, had the heart nett been taken out of them
by the unholy alliance contracted between the clergy and
the bookmakers when the Totalisator Bill was brought
before Parliament. In previous years, for weeks before
the racing carnival, a score of pulpits thundered simul-
taneously against the Curse of Gambling. This year the
parsons are dumb as oysters, being wretchedly aware
that they have placed themselves in a grotesquely false
position. The bookmakers have won a double in this
case. Tney got the help of the parsons against the
totalisator, and, by doing so, closed the mouths of their
allies in regard to their (the bookmakers’) own soul-
destroying trade. Taken altogether, it is a great year for
Ikey Mo.
♦ * ♦ ♦

Challenger, on Caulfield Cup day, was nartly excused by
a split hoof, or something. At the St. Alban’s lottery-
sale W. R. Wilson paid 500 guineas for the repossession of
this showy colt, notwithstanding its hollow back and non-
staying appearance.

In the old cemetery, Campbelltown (N.S.
Wales), is buried a woman whose tombstone re-
cords that she had eight children at a birth.
Ex-Premier Shiels, long time on the sick-
list, put in an appearance at the Vic. Assembly
last week and contributed to the praises of J. B.
Patterson.
Byron Miller, the
Launceston (Tas.) barri-
ster, was called out of
town once to defend a
girloharged with larceny.
She was an orphan and
served in a respected
family, in consideration
of which she was clothed
and fed. For some small
sin she was discharged,
and her room searched
by one of the ladies, who
unearthed a bundle of
linen tied up ready for
the road. The orphan
was given in charge and
stated to the police that
the Misses gave her
the linen for clusters.
Things were looking very
blue for the slavey, when
-some charitable person
sent for Miller. At the
enquiry the lawyer or-
dered the bundle to be
spread out, and various
mouldy - looking articles
were cl isplayed before
the admiring gaze of the
court. One “duster”
was a particularly dis-
reputable garment of
ladies’, underwear, and
this Miller dangled in
mid-air with much feel-
ing. “ Can anyone,” said
Byron solemnly, “ Can
anyone believe that such
a garment as this can
belong, or has ever be-
longed, to one of the
Musses——?” The ladies
would not identify and
the girl was discharged.

of Maoriland, the Boan-
erges of total prohibition,
is in London preparing
for a wrestle with the

penny papers are para-
graphing him as a man
of “splendid instincts”
with “ the presence of a
Dan O’Connell,” who

ences with a wild rush of

ing head and strong

A Sydney stipendiary, of most solemn and
ascetic mien, when O.P.S. down South was the
crack corner-man in the local nigger-minstrel
troupe.
George Dean will be 28 years old Nov. 14.
With good-conduct remissions he will, should he
live so long, be in his 39th year when released
from gaol—l9o6.
“Dicky” Speight’s return to Vic. Rail-
way Department depends solely on the decision
of the Full Court in the Age libel-case—so some
railway men assert.
Hori Grey’s Auckland admirers propose to
erect a statue in memory of the aged veteran.
The old man, however, would prefer the endow-
ment of a University scholarship.
A new C. of E. missionary diocese is to
be formed in New Guinea. It is to be hoped that,
for his own sake, the church authorities will
appoint a lean Bishop to the position.
Mayor Snowden welcomed Gov. Brassey
under a <£2so triumphal-arch erected by a corpora-
tion which thinks 4s. lid. a good day’s pay for a
man, with a horse and dray.
Beeronet Buxton was born within a few
months of the day that S.A. was proclaimed a
British province, and Baron Brassey was born in
the year that Melbourne was founded.
Gov. Buxton, it is said, was converted
from the drink-traffic at the same time as his
brewery. Buxton became a teetotaller, and the
brewery was converted into a company.
Dr. Andrew Garran, journalist and poli-
tician, is one of a family of 13 boys and girls.
Which perhaps accounts for the ill-luck of N.S.W.
Government Bills in the Upper House.
At Dromana (Yic.), where with her hus-
band she settled in 1841, the other day died Mrs.
Sarah Cann, aged 94. Her husband, who is still
hale and hearty, is 96.
Mark Twain turned up at the big Melb.
wool-sales one day last week, and “ remained an
interested spectator for half-au hour.” Feeling
the market before visiting a barber.
It was half expected that Gov. Brassey
would, on his own initiative, officially frown upon
certain Smelbourne land-boomsters if he got the
chance. He had the chance, at his first levee, and
missed it.
All the Governors of Australasia now are
either peers or closely related to the peerage :
Glasgow, Gormanston (who has just contrived to
sit down hard on a big fish-hook), Brassey, Hamp-
den, Lamington, Buxton (whose wife is an Earl’s
daughter) and Smith, who is an Earl’s cousin.

N.S.W. Judge Backhouse seems to have a
knack of getting into a mess on critical occasions.
He made a memorable mistake at the trial of the
Broken Hill rioters at Deniliquin, which mistake,
by the way, w'as hotly resented by Mr. Want,
who defended the prisoners.
Brewer Simms, one of the few S.A.
millionaires, travelled from England with Gov.
Buxton. They must have had many pleasant
chats about beer —that being the only connecting
link between them. For Simms is a sporting man
and knows nothing of Evangelicalism.
The N.S. v\. Attorney-General and the
Minister for Just-ice, in their defence of Justice
Backhouse’s release of attorney Rofe, dub that
criminal “ a lad.” Rofe is a married man and a
father. But then, as “Jack” Want said in the
Leg. Council, “ they start early m this country.”
Henry Palmer, the young fireman who
bravely carried the line ashore at the Wairarapa
wreck, was killed on a dredge in the Molyneaux
lately. Getting down from the water-tank, in-
stead of using the ladder he started over the
machinery and got jammed in the wheels, dying
in great agony.
It was a pretty and edifying sight at the
first Brassey levee to observe the purple-robed
R.C. Archbishop Carr and the academic-hooded
Presbyterian Moderator Rentoul chatting together
as amicably as if they bad never said a hard word
about each other in the newspapers. When the
augurs meet —they smile.
“I think we’ll start a matrimonial
agency,” remarked Hugh Taylor after having, at
the altar, given Mrs. Parkes No. 3 a smack on the
>heek like the drawing of a champagne-cork.
“Parkes and Taylor, Matrimonial Agents,” would
.ook well on a brass plate, Taylor finding the
capital, and P arkes the experience.
Gov. Brassey’s son-in-law, Freeman
Thomas, is an appropriate family connection, and
an indirect proof of the “golden Sunbeamer’s ”
good sense. Miss Brassey married into the con-
tract-business plutocracy, in which the original
Thomas and Freeman (partners) are big names.
That was better than buying a hungry earl.
W. T. Pinhe.y, Deputy Coroner, a N.S.
Wales settler of 60 years’ standing, died the
other day, aged 75. Mr. Pinhey succeeded to the
pharmacy business of Ambrose Eoss, who in turn
purchased from the notorious woman-poisoner,
John Tawell, the first man in England arrested
for murder by means of the electric telegraph.
Sydney Sunday Times penlady ;
I have been looking over his (ex-Preinier Parkes’) poems
to see whether he has addressed any to his present wife,
but can’t find even the slightest reference. Doubtless,
however, there are some unpublished poems.
The idea of looking over a writer’s poems to see
whether during his late wife’s lifetime he addressed
any poetry to his housemaid is indeed delicious.
A long journey by rail, even under the
most pleasant conditions, becomes a tedious affair.
It’s right enough for 3O miles or so, but,
when you have read all the comic papers, dis-
cussed every topic under the sun, political, social,
and athletic ; decided what is to be done with the
Upper House, lost a few shillings at nap, counted
the number of telegraph-poles to the mile, had a
smoke, lost a few shillings at 100, then you begin
to yawn and look round fora spot to stretch your-
self in for a godd sleep. Lucky you are if you
have brought a good rug wit’i you—such a one,
say, as Mark Foy, of Oxford-street, Sydney,
offers. Another excellent adjunct in a railway-
journey is a sound, substantial, stylish portman-
teau or Gladstone bag. You may be a great
traveller, but you will v travel far before you find
such value in bags and rugs as at Mark Toy’s.*
Ex-Premier Patterson, at the Yic. Com-
mercial Travellers’ Club, a few days ago :
“Permit me to say that we have become accus-
tomed to laugh at our political enemies, and when
you are prepared to laugh at your political
enemies, then you ought to be removed to some
better world.” And now the ex-Premier is dead.
Capt. Broomfield,
of Sydney, equally
well-known as a min-
ing man and as a
memberof the Marine
Board, is 74 years old
and sole survivor of
22 who gained a mas-
ter-mariner’s certifi-
cate with him in ’45.
He first came to Syd-
ney as third mate of
the Cressy, which
brought 300 convicts,
and he was the
means of saving the
vessel from the rocks
near the Cape, and
the convicts testi-
monialised him. One
of the convicts de-
veloped into a pros-
perous Melbourne
merchant. As mas-
ter of the Phoebe,
Capt. B. brought 300
Irish girls (all con-
victs), ranging from
17 to 70 years of age,
out to Hobart.
Though many were
very pretty, all be-
haved well coming
out—so says the ve-
teran captain. Going
up the Derwent the
lighter containing
them swamped, but
the third mate (they
had infused to get
into the lighter unless
he escorted them)
avoided any disaster
by employing the
whale-boats. Capt.
Broomfield has vivid
recollections of Syd-
ney in the early days
—of the Tank-stream,
then Sydney’.- water-
supply, flowing past
where the General
Post - office stands
now; of the penny
bridge spanning the
swamp between the
present Custom
House site and
George - street of
George - street itself
with scarcely a two-
storey building, and
thechain-gangs work-
ing in it all day long, and the bullock-teams
for tire interior reaching from the Royal Hotel to
Petersham, and the swearing thereof reaching to
the Blue Mountains. There were two churches
with preachers worth mentioning—nobody
cared. The Garandinis sang and danced at
the old Victoria, and the sign of British
justice was raised upon Gallows Hill. In
those days the aristocracy often rode to Home-
bush racecourse and back in carriages drawn by
dogs. Melbourne in ’47, when the Captain’s ship,
Sarah Scott, rodeall alone in Port Phillip, was so
embryonic that it hadn’t begun to smell. The
one wharf—Cole’s dock, just below the Falls—
was reached by a lighter. Gov. Latrobe gave th»
first Melbourne vice-regal ball at the opening oi
the first Melbourne arcade, and Capt. Broomtielr
attended in the spirit of a mariner bold, and ir
the get-up of a wild Indian. In ’54, the Oaptaii
took the first cargo of wool from Brisbane b
London in the ship Asa Packer. Brisbane ther
gave no sign or whisper of its present civilisatioi
—North Brisbane was a handful of houses ; Soutl
Brisbane a shanty or two ; the clear river flower
between. The Captain remembers the first go or
diggings at Ophir, near Orange, N.S.W., and wha
a day it was for the finder and the liar, for th-
oz. find and owt. report. He was by when G
A. Lloyd sold by auction the first gold—a lot
of 460 z. In Sydney Museum there is a sword-
fish which nearly settled the Captain’s ship as
she was coming from the Islands. Perhaps the
most adventurous act of the Captain’s life was to
bring the 20-ton Petrel from ’Frisco to Sydney
with one seaman and one passenger. The pas-
senger was Mr. Young, of the A.J.S. Bank,
brother to N.S.W. Works-Minister Young.

eye. What cheer, Brass-eye ?

A Fiji sugar-planter, on a visit to Sydney,
brought Ids black boy with him. He took him to
Mort’s Dock, and showed him the mighty
machinery at work ; he led him to the summit of
the Post-office, and pointed out the vast panorama
at his feet; he made him listen at the phonograph
to the playing of a brass band ; he let him press
the button to turn on the electric light of a big
building; but nothing of all the wonderful
achievements of science excited his surprise
except the clock of Delarue’s watch-shop in
George-street, the gong of which is manipulated
by the wooden figure of a negro. That appealed
to him. This stolidity is particularly characteris-
tic of the Chinese and Japanese races. They
refuse to be surprised. But a little Jap who
strolled into Mark Toy’s the other day was very
much astonished when some beautiful Japanese
white silk, 23 and 27 inches wide, was set before
him at Is. o|d. and Is. 4|d. a yard. His dark
eyes opened in an incredulous manner, for not
even in Japan had he ever seen such value.*

Uo»n the street, exclaiming “ Brass h’Eye!

When the Reid Ministry falls it will pro-
bably be Attorney-General Want or Minister
Gould, or both together, who will have smashed it.
Colonial Secretary Buckley, of Maoriland,
is an advocate of a State liquor monopoly. Un-
luckily, Buckley is too mild and.beautiful to advo-
cate anything very seriously.
Melb. evening paper supplies two items
re the demise of J. B. Patterson. “His place
can never be filled.” . , “A large number of
candidates will contest the vacant seat.”

THE REAL MACKAY.
MR. M‘MURPHY (licensee of the Deadman’s Trade Hotel, Yalgoo,
stands on the cerandah of his pub. and challenges all
creation to mortal combat): “ Come on, ye curs! The
man that won’t fight after drinking three glasses of MY
rum must be a coward at heart I”
Carey’s assertion: “The country, which limits
itself to the export of raw material must end by
exporting its men.”
Most of the city letters to S.A. provincial
journals are written from the 11 kgister office, and
the result is that outsiders get the idea that there
is a consensus of metropolitan opinion adverse to
Premier Kingston and his Government.
Premier Reid now asserts that the N.S. W.
Council has no right to amend the Land and
Income-tax Assessment Bill. Just four months
ago the same wobbler said that it iiad the right,
and that the Assessment Bill had been sent up
separate from the actual taxation bill expressly
that the Council might exercise that right.
Three members of the Vic. Cabinet, Pea-
cock, Taverner, and Foster, may be considered to
have preserved their self-respect by car. fully stay-
ing away from every meeting held to consider the
fate of Emma Williams,-and leaving the job chiefly
to the lawyers of the 1 Cabinet. At time of wilt-
ing, The Bulletin does not know whether a post-
mortem was held to settle the question of the
Woman’s pregnancy, nor whether, if such a post-
mortem was held, it was attended by any but the
official doctors.
Governors Laniington, Hampden, Buxton,
and Brassey were all members of the Imperial
Federation League of 1888. The simultaneous
convergence of these streams of influence on Aus-
tralia may reasonably be taken as the outcome of
a deliberate imperial purpose. No doubt Gerard
Smith’s heart is imperially in the right place, and
thus Australia is, or will be when the last of this
band arrives, wholly, so far as its highest offices
are concerned, in the control of one particular
British league. Furthermore, the league of ’BB
affirmed that Australian Federation would bring
Imperial Federation one step nearer, and hence
the converging streams of the present possess in-
creased significance.
Almost the last parliamentary statement of
J. B. Patterson, leader of Vic. Opposition, was to
assure Chief-Secretary Peacock that he would lend
a hand to help through the bill for the repression
of sweating. Patterson was a bad politician, a
dunderheaded Minister, and a violent office-
hungerer, but, anyhow, his last political utterance
was worthy of a much wiser man.
One hideous miscarriage of justice follows
another in N.S.W. with startling succession. And
all these gross iniquities go practically unrebuked
and unredressed by Parliament, simply because
the Labor-party has in its mouth a sponge filled
w.thmilk of the cocoanut, and is bound to sup-
port the lawyer-bossed Govt, through thick and
thin. The Legal Ring—that is the real Govern-
ment.
The unkindest thing said about the late
Vic. ex-Premier Patterson was contained in
Premier Turner’s well-meant speech of eulogy.
“ I regret to notice,” said Turner, “ that the ranks
of the men who have worked hard to make the
province what it is are being rapidly thinned.”
Fancy putting that epitaph over your friend’s
grave : “ Here lies a man who worked hard to
make Victoria what it is ! ”

Apropos of the case of
Role, the Sydney solicitor
found guilty of conspiracy
and released under the First
Offenders’ Act, this is more
or less interesting. A day
or two after Rofe’s dis-
charge a man was found
guilty at a Sydney police
court of stealing some small
articles. He had previously
borne an excellent char-
acter ; also, he was out of
work, hungry, stone-broke,
and had a wife to main-
tain, and he stole the goods
in question apparently to
raise a little money for their
maintenance. He also asked
to be released under the
First Offenders’ Act, but
was promptly squashed.
“The Act was never meant
to apply to you, but to
youthful offenders,” said
the Court, and the sentence
was four months’ “ hard.”
Another proof of the dis-
advantage of not being a
lawyer.
After waiting many
days, during which it was
engaged in preparing itself
for investigation, Melb.
Crown Law Department
has announced that a Board
will be appointed to en-
quire into Mr. Lormer’s
indictment of the honorary
magistracy, and that the
onus of proof will rest on
Lormer. As a detective
has been constantly em-
ployed for months examin-
ing the evidence available,
it is generally assumed that
he has reported that it is
not sufficiently formidable
to make enquiry dangerous
or sufficient to save Lormer
from being placed in a false position.
The Seddon Government has suffered many
defeats of late, and its big majority is evidently
becoming demoralised. Its following still amounts
to three fourths of the Assembly, but it seems to
be growing weary of party-government, and only
follows when it thinks it right to do so. And, un-
luckily, Government by Contradiction is based
wholly on the assumption that the party shall
always follow, whether it thinks it right to do so
or not. A Legislature with a conscience makes
the party-system wholly unworkable.
The Nelson (Q.) Govt, started its career
by wielding the axe of reform in three directions.
1. Retrenchment (unsparing) in the Civil-service.
2. Unsparing reduction of Education Vote and raising
of school a^e.
8. Abjuring of political railways and similar abomina-
tions.
But within the last six months the service has
been placated, the school age lowered, political
railways promised all over the country, and every-
body is happy again.
Australians seem unaware that the Northern
Territory of South Australia (one-sixth, and by no
means the worst sixth, of this continent), which a
British company—chiefly the South African gang
—propose to acquire under charter, is “in no wise
an Australian possession.” It belongs to J. Bull,
and is “only being administered by South Aus-
tralia in the Imperial interest.” The company,
which calmly proposes to run the rule over a fifth
of Australia, has been at work in Adelaide for the
last five months, and includes the Rothschilds and
the inevitable Col. North. Now for Federation !
