The lute J. B. PATTLERSOX, ex-Premier of Victoria.
Labor-member Hancock, Vic., had his
feelings hurt the other night by an accusation of
disloyalty : Salmon, M.P., having solemnly in-
formed Vic. Assembly that the member for Foots-
oray refused to rise when “the Queen ” was pro-
posed on the arrival of Gov. Brassey. Hancock
was shocked at the “cowardly charge,” and
excitedly explained that he had not risen to drink
the toast because he had no liquor to drink it
with, but when the liquor was supplied he drank
the Queen “ with great gusto.” Hancock is now
admired as a devoted loyalist who would risk any
ignominy in preference to drinking the “Throne
and Person ” in fresh air.
Ex-Premier Patterson, who died last week,
was the scientific trimmer. Even a trimmer can-
not help having convictions, and Patterson’s were
invincibly Tory, all through his political life. He
began by trying to get in on that racket, under
\,, nrT , „ : • , c
f A * T *’ m tl,e days of Maokmnon the
* lrst - Nofc “Sing able to manage it, he played
fcbe M'Culloch kind of game. He ended in a
genuine way—as the Tory. Had he stuck to
his . convictions throughout life, like Murray
Sm,th > Service, or Berry, he would have been a
powerful man, but he leaves only a reputation of
the somewhat improved Colonel Boots Smith
description. It could be shown that Patterson
ar gued both for and against, in regard to every
£ reat question that has agitated Victoria,
W.: “In Patterson, there were the makings
of a really splendid man. He had nearly all the
essentials of success—insight, pluck, vigorous
humor, cordiality, and any amount of oommon-
sense. But with it there was too much levity, too
much love of the glitter of things, too much
adaptability and short-sighted self-seeking. Demo-
cratie, and gifted with Higinbotham’s directness
of purpose and abhorrence of sham, he would have
been one of the greatest of Australia’s statesmen,
Variable, unreliable, and only a plutocrat in sin-
centy and truth, the people weighed him in the
balance and found him wanting.”
Another interesting event looms
in royal circles. Another Fife to be
added to Queen Victoria’s German
band.
Apropos of nothing. The
very latest definition of “ lady-help ”
is “one who helps herself to her
mistress’s shoes.”
Suicide seems to be coming into
fashion with the British aristocracy.
A Duke of Bedford, an Earl of
Shaftesbury, and a Marquis of
Waterford have wiped themselves out
within the last few years. The latter
was the son of “ wild Waterford,”
whose practical jokes in the forties
and fifties were practical proofs of
insanity.
Editor Bulletin, —Re alligator
discussion. Maryborough Chroni-
cle (Q.) records that on Sept. 30 an
alligator (I beg pardon—a crocodile)
was seen near the mouth of the Mary
by the occupants of the local Govt,
launch. The brute had been seen on
previous occasions (according to the
Chronicle), and its existence appears
to be well authenticated. I’m afraid
Mr. Hedley, like myself, will have to
slightly modify his Southern boun-
dary.—P. Luftig.
One of-the largest vessels on the
Australian coast (the Banffshire) re-
cently unloaded at Townsville wharf
—wherefore Townsville jubilates,
though all the rest of the shipping
was delayed till the big visitor went.
At the same time, small coasting
steamers are only allowed to drop
their cargo on the wharfs per medium
of lighters. So much Townsville
capital has been sunk in the “lighter ”
business that vessels cannot be allowed
to dispense with these little adjuncts
all at once.

LE ROI S’AMUSE.
The latest English files give us particulars of
the reason of the late general change in Australian
Governors. It seems that Queen Victoria was
worrying a good deal about the lack of “Jingoism’
on the part of the Australian provinces, and one
day she was standing at Lord Salisbury’s side on
the look-out tower of Balmoral Castle, lost in deep
meditation, even the glorious panorama unveiled
at her feet, which is one of the most picturesque
scenes of the world, having no attraction for her.
Suddenly throwing off her despondent mood she
turned round to her companion and said : “Sally,
old boy, the horizon looks gloomy, the silken bonds
with Australia are getting very slack, let us revive
things there by sending out a new lot of
Governors, that will make things 4 hum.’ The
Kangaroolanders are depressed after the bursting
of their various booms, things are only middling,
and the colonists need some distraction.” The
result of this conversation was the appointment of
Viscount Hampden for N.S.W., Lord Brassey
for Victoria. Sir T. E. Buxton for S.A., and
Colonel Smith for Westralia. These nominees
had a meeting and decided to do their utmost to
make the Australians forget their troubles, to
encourage sports, but also to preach thrift as a
means to re-establishing prosperity. They decided
that horse-racing should be discouraged, and to
introduce to both sexes the exhilarating sport of
bicycling in its place. Thrift is to be encouraged
by this means, as they consider the healthy excite-
ment derived from bicycle-riding better than the
glamor of horse-racing at nothing like the expense.
They also decided to advise colonists to drink
less and to smoke less noxious tobaccos, but to
smoke what is now considered the one smoke of
the world, viz., Frossard’s Cavour Cigars. Eng-
lish experience has proved them to be a cheap,
uninjurious enjoyment, unsurpassed anywhere,
and obtainable everywhere in packets of 8 for Is.*
Prof. Loisette, the “assimilative-memory”
man, announces by advfc. in this issue that he is
giving lessons in his system of never forgetting
tilings. Any person who is cursed with a bad
memory and wants it repaired is invited to com-
municate with him and try his system, which has
now been in use for a quarter of a century.
Parsons, the Vic. cyclist who shone so
brightly against Zimmerman, is 10st. weight
stripped, and 21 years old —the age when a man
can sprint and sprint and sprint. He started
path-racing about two years ago, and was thought
a good thing for his first Austral, so remarkably
well had he been riding, but nervousness paralysed
him before the race. He won eight races, ranging
from half to two miles, before last Dec. ; then he
won the 10-mile Vic. Championship, and broke
the world’s grass record with 26min. 15sec. A
few days later he lowered all Australian records
for half-mile upwards to 10 miles ; the track was
the Melbourne Exhibition track; the 10 miles’
time was 23min. 58sec. Last Sep, he won the
Vic. 50 Mile Championship Road Race, and the
Victory Road Race from scratch. At last
Adelaide meeting (before the Zimmerman debut)
he won the half-mile and 10-mile scratch races,
beating Megson and Lewis. He has met Megson
in five scratch races altogether, beaten him twice
in a 10-mile and once in a half-mile race, and been
beaten by Megson in a one-mile championship on
Melbourne cricket-ground and in a five-mile at
Brisbane. He thinks that Zimmerman, fit and
well, is a notch above any Australian. Parsons
rides a Humber.

The last English election was a pronuncia-
mento against Church Disestablishment in the
unholy alliance of rum and religion. The fact is
the Anglican Church has become so colorless that
it embraces a huge majority of the unbelievers,
who take a pride in its age, and its architecture
and wealth and respectability. Even the upper-
crust of Nonconformism supports the ecclesiastical
establishment. Atheist Moncure Conway wrote an
elaborate magazine article in support of the en-
dowed Church of England.
John Baptist, a “gentleman ob color,”
was “ had ” before the eternal North Melb. court
the other day to show cause why he should not
pay for the maintenance of an illegitimate, which
a young woman alleged against him. Lawyer for
defence: “Complainant has no case, your Wor-
ships—she has no witnesses.” Complainant, with
a shriek ; “ Aint I, though ? Look at this. He’s
a black man.” And she held up a black-and-tan
infant for the inspection of the court, and to lend
color to her story. Case postponed—possibly to
give the baby time to bleach.
Dear Bulletin, —lie the Dean poisoning.
About ’B7, Surgeon-major Cross was tried, con-
victed and executed at Cork, Ireland, for poison-
ing his wife ; object—to marry the governess. It
was proved that Cross for weeks had given his
wife small doses of arsenic, to which, as it did
not work speedily enough, he at length added
strychnine with fatal results. The analysis was
conducted by C. Yelverton Pearson, M.D., now
Prof, at Dublin University; Prof. Rawdon
Macnamara, M.D., watched on behalf of the
defence. The monstrous theory of the counter-
acting effects of the strychnine was not even
hinted at. This case should be known to the
Australian faculty. Cross drew suspicion on him-
self by burying his victim at 6 a.m. and issuing a
certificate of death due to typhoid. Your remarks
that two analysts are necessary in a case of life
and death are strictly to the point.— Atragene.
A Bulletin correspondent :—So Geo.
Clarke, who with Mosman and Frazer dis-
covered the “Towers,” is gone, clubbed to
death by a New Guinea nigger while prospecting.
His end was, in a way, worthy of his life. A wild,
adventurous life, and a wild, adventurous death.
Well known in the North was Clarke, and widely
and deservedly popular. The Russell River gold-
field was opened up by this intrepid pioneer, and
had he lived there is little doubt that he would
have laid bare much of the auriferous wealth of
New Guinea. No man knew the Australian
blacks better than Clarke—much of his success as
an explorer being due to this circumstance, but he
appears to have been hardly circumspect enough
with the more formidable savages of Papua. His
discoveries enriched others, but never himself.
Like Nash, of Gympie, he did not possess the
true grab-faculty—your daring, ad venturous spirit
seldom does. He received no honors—no decora-
tions—no tinsel baubles; he died as he lived, a
simple, sturdy Man —grit to the backbone.

Under the new Belgian law bachelors oyer
25 have one vote, married men and widowers with
families two votes, priests and professional men
three votes. Severe penalities are inflicted tor
failure to vote.
“Women are the source of Life, and for
that reason they should be on terms of perfect
equality with men, and descent would be safer
traced through the mother than the father, as
there can be no possible doubt regarding the
maternal parentage.”—Rideb Haggaed.
North roef, off the western side of Capri-
corn Channel, has a lighthouse. The little sand-
bank where the lighthouse is situate ; d is resorted
to by birds and turtles which deposit their eggs.
Gulls and sea-birds are fed regularly, and respond
to the call of the superintendent’s wife when she
beats a metal spoon on a dish —just as barn-door
fowls would do.
The late manager of Melbourne Mutual
Store, finding obscenities written in the lava-
tories, posted notices setting forth that owing to
the disfigurement of the walls no bonus would be
paid to any employe that quarter. Matters at
once regulated themselves, and at next bonus-time
the walls were clean.

BNO A BLESSING IN EVERY HOUSE. -
ENO’S “FRUIT SALT.”-A lady writes:—
“ Everything, medicine or food, ceased to act
properly for at least three months before I com-
menced taking it; the little food I could take
generally punished me or returned. My life was
one of great suffering, so that I must have suc-
cumbed before long. To me and our family it has
been a great earthly blessing.’’ Sold by all
chemists and stores.

for unregistered dogs, and at the pub. he met the
State-schoolmaster. After sundry drinks—Peda-
gogue : “I’ve—hie —spent this week’s fees.’

THE BULLAPAN.
The bullapan is out at night,
As nimble as of yore,
The children tremble with affright
To hear the monster roar ;
He sports a long and shaggy coat,
There’s frenzy in his eye—
A relic, he, of days remote
When piggies used to fly.

On SATURDAY, 9th NOVEMBER, 1895, the
Good -Sheds at Kedfern and Alexandria will be
closed ; Darling Harbor Goods-Shed will be open
until 10 a.m. for the delivery of perishables, and

perishables only.
The Goods-Sheds at other Stations will be closed
except for the receipt and delivery of perishables,
Handbills and further particulars can he ob-
tained from all Station Masters and at the Central
Booking-office, George-street, Sydney.

The Sydney cash cyclist was in all his glory on
Zimmei man arriving, and abstracted from his guinea
subscription-fee about 7s. Od. in drink. Zim., hardly
sipping ins champagne and looking careworn and ill, was
nevertheless drowned in the soda of the cash cyclists’
speeches.
Altogether, the chances this trip are against
Zim. reaching form, hut his methods of training will
doubtless bring him to the post as well as seven out of
ten Australian riders.
• • * •
Zimmerman says that the cash-system purifies
amateurism. “ Riders never race so well and so straight
as when the money is at the last lap’s end.”
Off the track Zimmerman is a well-dressed,
unassuming, affable Yank—readier to listen than to talk,
and willing to talk about anything except himself. On
the track lie looks a game-eyed fibrous athlete, and his fibre
se ms to be of better texture than that of any known
Australian cyclist.
Walford, Zim.’s manager, denies that he
asked Parsons to forsake the Humber for the Nim-
rod. It was inopportune for Zim., Parsons defeating
him on a Humber. The Humber, it is claimed, can point
to more Australian successes than all other machines put
together.
* * • *
“ Wally ” Kerr, the crack New South Wales
amateur cyclist, the fastest of all Australian cyclists
behind a tandem, is training steadily to lower all Austra-
lian cycling records from quarter-mile up to 10 miles ; then
lie goes for the 41. L. championships.
* • • •
Parsons has defeated other champions by the
same tactics as those successfully employed by him
against Zimmerman, viz., forcing the pace from the start.
In the tO-mile Championship he smothered his field before
half the race was over, and did the same thing in Adelaide
a few weeks ago when he won by over two furlongs in five
miles.
• • • •
Zimmerman never rode upon a grass-track until
in Australia. The S.idney cricket-ground grass track is,
lie says, good, but thereon the exhibition sprint is im-
possible.
• * • •
The machine of Matthews, Melb. cyclist, must
have stood upon its hind-wheel and laughed at the
240yds. start allotted by Osborne, the Sydney League
handicapper, to Matthews in the two miles. Sccft,
another Melnnurnite, with 220yds., is also treated well.
If Matthews doesn’t go for this, he will either make or
unmake his friends as dead bird for the Austral.
» • » •
Payne, a .£lOO winner at last Sydney League
cycle carnival, is back on SOyds. this time, but he has much
improved the last 12 mouths.
* * * •
How Anglophile is the cable-service may be
judged from the fact that the defeat of the English ath-
letes at New York was ascribed by it to the hot w eather ;
whereas the mail-news shows that the leading Englishmen
admitted they were fairly beaten.
• • • *
Stern and stately Zimmerman is seldom ob-
served laughing. The only hearty laugh he has indulged
in since landing occurred at Adelaide, when he turned up
to see the local talent play the ’Murkan game of baseball.
• • ♦ *
The first Melb. Cup stake didn’t include any-
thing for the second horse, and it wasn’t until eight years
after establishment that the third was given £2O.
* * * *
Zim. says that a cyclist is never so brilliant as
at 21, when he sprints on the wines of enthusiasm. After
that he rides with his head, hands, and legs, gauges his
opponent, and challenges him at the inferred proper
moment.
Zim. riding sits well forward, over his work.
All the Australian riders note it and shift their saddles
forward. When Zim. heat England, the riders did as the
Australians are now doing.
Racing, if Zim. collapses, he falls as a whole.
His ai ms, his legs, his wind are gone—he is spread-eagled
entirely.
It will have been noticed that Perkins, the
M.C.C., and Alcoek, the Surrey C.C., secretaries, in
selecting the best English team, choose respectively but
three and four of St odd irt's late eleven. Perkins’s eleven
includes Stod lart, Richardson and Peel, Alcock’s these
three and A. Ward.

At the annual sports meeting of the Ranleigh
(Eng.) Harriers Dr. W. J. M. Harry threw the 171 b. ham-
mer 135 ft. 6in., only 2ft. 2sin. short of his recoid nearly
as far again as J. A. Reid, N.S.W. champion, threw on
Eight Hours Day at Sydney.

A. W. Fisher
(Kati Kati):
Yourself and friend were
probably “had” foryourone
pound sterling and fourpence.
Anyway, you deserved to he had. It is
years since the genuine J. J. Miller ran
sweeps. About a year ago he attempted to re-
vive his ancient glories, hut the Melb. police
swooped down upon him and seized ttie cash in hand.
There re bogus Millers and bogus Adamses all over the
place. You seem to have struck a very palpable fraud.

Mr. H. J. Samuell, the well-known music
publisher, has just issued a guide to Sydney which
will be found very useful by visitors. It contains,
in a small space, much valuable information about
everything in the city and its vicinity which a
stranger wotdd desire to see and how to get there,
and it is illustrated with pictures of the various
beauty spots in and around the N.S. W. capital.
As a means whereby the visitor may avoid getting
lost in a badly tangled city this little volume can
be highly recommended.*

SUCH WERE THE OBSERVATIONS
OF THE DELIGHTED HUNDREDS
AS THE Y PASSED OUT ON SATUR-
DAY NIGHT.
Bead short Extracts from lengthy Press Notices of
Sunday Papers , and Monday's Commercial
Papers, the Herald and Daily Telegraph
Vide HERALD :—“ The hall is spacious, well
lighted and ventilated, and perfectly equipped
with the adjuncts of a Music-hall; and, granted
that the entertainments given in it are always up
to the standard of the initial one, it should have a
long run of popularity. A conspicuous feature of
the entertainment was the excellence of the
Orchestration, consisting of ten instruments.”
Vide DAILY TELEGRAPH Mr. Lawler
opened the Hall under most favourable auspices as
regards the entertainment and the attendance.
The almost inevitable defects of an initial perfor-
mance appeared to be absent. The rain even did
not interfere with the attendance, for the notice,
‘Full House,’ was up just as the programme
started. The rise of the curtain disclosed a pretty
set backed by a landscape. Mr. W. A. Ball was
interlocutor, Mr. Horace Bent and Mr. Jack
Williams were the bones, and Mr. Sam Rowley
and Mr. Gus Franks the tambos. A highly suc-
cessful start has been made by Mr. Lawler at his
new hall. Situated as it is in the heart of the
popular part of the city it is certain to become a
favourite resort with the large and growing num-
bers of playgoers who prefer the lighter forms of
amusement which the well-conducted music-hall
affords.”
Vide SUNDAY TIMES “ The opening show
was highly creditable to all concerned. The
building is well adapted for shows of the kind,
and if last night’s experience is a criterion the
Sydney Music-hall has entered on a lengthy cat eer
of prosperity.”
Vide TRUTH “ Happily situated in George-
street, and within speaking distance of the Town-
hall, artistically furnished, carefully built under
the s pervision of an architect who thoroughly
understands acoustics, the pretty hall should prove
a great favourite with the naturally pleasure-
loving people of New South Wale?. Immediately
the curtain rose last night, and disclosed an artis-
tically grouped company, fringed with the ever-
popular black fringe of tambos and tones, a feeling
of satisfaction went through everyone present.
The venture was going with a bang. One could
see it, and the band soon drove that feeling home
with telling effect.”
Vide BULLETIN ■“ If the new enterprise
keeps on as it has begun it ought to fill a long-
felt want, or something to that effect.”

Warrnambool (Vic.) Murray, M.L.A., in
reference to the salaries of taxidermists at Melb.
Museum :—“ As those useless scientists with the
extraordinary names must now have stuffed all
the beasts, birds, and fish in Australia, they
should be set to work to stuff each other, and then
jjhe expenditure would immediately cease.”
An English soldier who had lost his nose
has obtained a new one by having one of his
fingers grafted into the flesh where the bridge of
the nose should be. After it had been kept there
a month by a plaster of Paris bandage, the finger
was amputated and trimmed into shape, and a
flap of skin from the forehead brought down over
it. Now he has a very passable-looking nose with
a good bony bridge, a thing few manufactured
noses can boast.
A Chow converted to Christianity by a
R.C. mission learns that “Protestantism is an
immoral practice, which blasphemes God, degrades
man, and endangers society.” The leper bagged
by the direct opposition party is notified that “ the
church has no foe comparable to the Jesuit,”
whilst the various chapel missionaries are unani-
mous in nothing but their scorn for the older
Kerristian faiths. Converts as a rule grab one
another by the pigtail and scream “Heletic!”
“No Plopery ! ” or “God blessum Johnny
Wesley !” as the case may be. A Chow Baptist
has even been known to pray for the conversion of
the misguided missionary who lured his mother
into the Anglican church, and it is dimly sus-
pected that the Ku Cheng “Vegetarians” got
their message of salvation from a Plymouth
blother of the most extreme type.
They were at crib one night in Coolgardie
—a time when the jackeroo holds forth on all sub-
jects under the sun, especially concerning what
his people would think if they could only see him
as he was. The conversation suddenly and with-
out provocation turned from nothing in particular
to whether Mayor Shaw thoroughly grasped the
position of affairs of which he was the supposed
head, when a venturesome spirit suggested that
“ he ought to, as he had been Mayor of Adelaide.
“Nothing of the kind,” blurted jackeroo know-
all; “I saw in the paper that he was only ex-
Mayor of Adelaide.”
Dear Bulletin, — I met an Englishman in
Tasmania the other day, a younger member of a
“ good ” family, whom I would not mind backing
for a “bit” to give your “Dipsomaniac” two
stone and a beating. I travelled in the train with
him from Hobart to Launceston. He left Hobart
possessed of a flask of brandy, and flung the
empty flask out of the window ere reaching the
half-way house at Parattah, where he had a
bottle of wine for “eleven-o’clock lunch.” Be-
tween Parattah and Launceston he polished off
a flask of whisky neat, washing down dinner
at Launceston with another bottle of wine.
During the afternoon he strolled round and
sampled a few nips of ’Bl “old rye,” which
a local spirit-merchant had been boasting about
on the train - ride. Another bottle of wine
with his evening meal, and, sober as a judge in
legs and brain, he strolled down town to buy a
paper. As luck would have it, a child in the shop
was playing wdth a toy crocodile, which had just
been wound up and was crawling along the floor.
With a yell that could have been heard the other
side of the island our hero fled to his hotel, where
it took a couple of men to hold him down until he
slumbered, some hours later. Next morning he
turned up at breakfast clean-shaved and fresh as
paint, by far the brightest man at the table.
What sort of a champion is he ? —W.

Dear Sir,—ln the year of 18711 was eight days m the water sheep-
washing, and contracted rheumatism and stiffness in the joints, and
have tried all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney I had a

try your Remedies. On Thursday before Good Friday I took the first
of your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Special Powerful Alfaline
Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with

years ago. I can with great recommend your Remedies to
anyone suffering from the same complaint. May you long be spared
under God to be a blessing to suffering humanity.

Great Distribution of Sydney City and Suburban Freehold Properties.
First Prize, £28,500 ; Second Prize, £21,000 ; Third Prize, £16,000; Fourth Prize, £11,250 ; Fifth Prize, £7OOO.
500 Prizes, valued at £150,000,
AMONGST SHAREHOLDERS IN THE EQUITABLE BUILDING SOCIETY, BRISBANE (INCORPORATED ACCORDING TO ACT OF PARLIAMENT).
Committee of Management—Hon. B. B. MORETON ; Hon. JOHN DONALDSON ; ACHESON OYEREND, Esq., C.E. Sydney Board of Reference— BßUCE SMITH Esq. •
J. E. BURNS, Esq. ; W. J. GREEN, Esq. Bankers—THE COMMERCIAL BANKING COMPANY OF SYDNEY. ’
After considerable negotiations, the Committee of Management have succeeded in purchasing upon the most advantageous terms from the Colonial Finance, Mortgage, Investment and Guarantee
Corporation, Limited, Sydney, a considerable portion of their valuable Freehold Properties, which they have decided to distribute by ballot amongst the members of the Society, as provided by the Society’s
lEI quitaljle
rules.
The Eq.uita.lDle Building Society, Urialoane (Incorporated according to Act of Parliament). Proposed Issue of £150,000 Shares of £l.
The Distribution will be as under : — 5OB Prizes, arranged in value from £2,0 to £28,500, in classes as follows :
First Prize. £28,500. 2nd prize, £21,000. 3rd prize, £16,000. 4th prize, £11,250. sth prize, £7OOO. 6th prize, £6500. 7th prize, £4BOO. Bth prize, £4650. 9th prize, £4350. 10th prize £400&
Hth prize, £3750. 12th prize, £3500. 13th prize, £2500. 14th prize, £2OOO. 15th prize, £l5OO. 2 prizes, each £1250. 1 prize, £B5O. 1 prize, £750. 9 prizes, each £5OO. 1 prize, £3lO. 10 prizes each
£250. 6 prizes, each £2OO. 4 prizes, each £l5O. 26 prizes, each £lOO. 4 prizes, each £75. 69 prizes, each £5O. 35 prizes, each £4O. 10 prizes, each £35. 109 prizes, each £3O. 206 prizes, each £2O.
Each Shareholder, tor every share held by him, will have 30 separate chances, viz., a chance of drawing 30 separate prizes with a TOTAL VALUE of £125,910, so that an Investor of £1 may secure
properties of the value of £125,910.
Ballot, according to rules, will be held on Friday, 20th December, 1895, at Brisbane, under the
supervision of the Committee of Management and representative citizens.
George Adams, Esq. (“ Tattersall ”), has generously placed his services and his complete Ballot
Apparatus at the disposal of the Committee.
Intending Subscribers should apply on form below, and also enclose two addressed stamped
envelopes, one of which will be used to return Share Certificate, and the other to send result of ballot;
they should also register their letters, and, until acknowledged, hold Post-office receipt.
The mode of Drawing will be by numbered marbles, placed in a revolving barrel, from which will
be first drawn the numbers securing the 206 £2O prizes. The marbles drawn will then be replaced,
and the numbers will be drawn securing the 109 .£3O prizes, and so on for the several classes of prizes
successively, until all the prizes have been allotted.
Thus every Share ensures each holder a chance of each of the several classes of prizes (30 in
all), so that every share gives a chance of obtaining 30 separate prizes, with a total value of -£125,910,
making it possible for £1 to win £125,910 worth of property.
Should the total number of Shares not be applied for, the Committee reserve power under articles
and terms of Conditional Purchase to enable them to ensure an allotment, and to modify scheme and
schedule of prizes accordingly.
Clear Titles for the properties to he distributed as Prises are guaranteed to this Society,
Transfers may be obtained by successful Shareholders within one month from date of ballot.
Free of any deduction for commission.
The Ballot will absolutely take place at Brisbane, on Friday, December 20,1895,

The Committee of Management desire to draw the particular attention of all intending Subscribers
to the excellence of the properties, which have been most carefully selected by the Directors of the
Colonial Finance Corporation in Sydney, and disposed of to the Building Society, by a Conditional
Sale, at Less than Cost Price.
It will be observed that nearly.all the principal properties are of a Commercial nature, and Rent-
producing, and though the rentals have been greatly reduced to meet the existing depression, there is
little room for doubt that at an early date considerably increased rentals will be received.
Even at present rentals a handsome yearly income will accrue to the fortunate drawer of any of
the larger rent-producing properties, should he elect to retain same in preference to disposing of it.
It is now being generally recognised throughout the Colonies that the City of Sydney is destined
to become the Commercial Emporium of Australasia, and the fact of the above properties being nearly
all situated in or about Sydney renders the present Distribution more substantially attractive than
any previously offered to the public.
Detailed particulars of properties have been carefully prepared, and can be seen at the offices of
the Society, or at the Sydney Agency, by any intending Shareholder.

P. 0.0. preferred. If cheque sent exchange must be added; if postal notes from other colonies sent, poundage must

The Hundredth Chance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Someone says thorn should be no work the day
after a holiday,’’ c- : I Mr. Byrne.
“I think a*l i i .try teachers should get an
extra week to mak : up for the lonely, homesick
days after the holidays are over,” said Miss
Murray. “ Isn’t it an awful feeling ? Ah, well,
we’ve had good weather and a very good time.
Monday will come soon enough without thinking
of it. I’ll have to leave on Saturday—there’s no
train on Sunday. I wouldn’t mind going back so
much if one could start working right away. It’s
the dismal Sunday I dread. I’ll start moping and
be miserable all the week—and I wanted to stay
in Sydney so much. Isn’t it a shame ? ”
“ Well, wait and go up by Monday night’s
train,” said Mr. Byrne.
“ But suppose the inspector should go there ? ”
“Not he ! You’ve got to chance meeting him ;
but they’re not supposed to examine for a month
after the holidays. It’s safe enough. Tell the
people you got a day off; mark the youngsters
present; and who’s to know ? Pooh I I’ve done it
often.”
“Do you really think it’s safe?” asked Miss
Murray.
“Of course,” answered Mr. Byrne; “it’s a
hundred to one chance against being caught.”
Miss Murray arrived at her school on Tuesday
morning, and, being very young in deceit, at once
marked the roll for the previous day. During the
morning her inspector drove up. “ All who were
present yesterday when the roll was called,
stand ! ” said he, as he opened the register.
Not a movement.
“Scholars, dismiss, and go home. Kindly
gather any personal effects you may have here,
Miss Murray. I might mention that I waited
here two hours yesterday. Thanks. Good morn-
ing.
It was the hundredth chance !
PONTE.
According to Gov. M‘Gregor, of New
Guinea, the mission-station at Dobu is teaching a
most valuable lesson. The missionary-students
are trained to do, at the same time, their preach-
ing and their share of manual labor, their gardens
being actually the best and most productive in the
village. If somebody would only educate the
Australian clergy up to the same lofty ideal !
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
The last war-correspondent to visit Tas.
has been telling the Maorilanders what a beauti-
ful spot the little island will make as an out-
station for the savage invader. But he didn’t
tell the Tasmen so. The military force of
Tasmania, is chiefly composed of officials and
a battery which is as innocent as it looks.
Apropos : The late Thomas Field, M.L.C.,
of Westfield, was showing the beauties of the
River Tamar to a distinguished visitor. “ Pig
Island-r-our northern battery,” said the hon. Tom.
“Ah ! really,” said the D.V., scanning the bare
shores of Pig through his field-glasses, “ but I
don’t see the guns.” “Not such blanky fools,”
said Tom, “ we hide ’em in the bulrushes.”
The demand for “ Crom-a-boo ” has neces-
sitated a fourth edition, which is now in the press.*
Professional thieves gather some of their
most valuable information from the daily press,
per favor of the police. Under the heading of
“ Recent Robberies ” they find paragraphs such
as this : —“ On Sunday evening the house of Mrs.
—-, widow, street, Jamberoo, was visited
by thieves, who forced the back window in and
stole clothing to the value of £5 10s. Mrs,
had in the house at the time a valuable parcel of
jewellery , which the thieves missed,” The policeman
who gives such information, and the journalist
responsible for its appearance in print, deserve 14
days apiece for “inciting to crime.” Practically
the Artful Dodger who got away with the widow’s
raiment is advised to revisit the scene of his
depredation and look for the jewellery.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
A i i Is’ Materials; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a .!-panese Exhibition; 12, a Wonderland;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.*
Visitor (in a Murray district): “Nowi
butcher, when you are dressing a diseased beast
for sale, what do you do with the cancerous part B
you cut out?” Guileless slaughterman: “Feed
’em to the pigs : it don’t hurt ’em ! What else
would you do with ’em ? ”

After the Accident.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ONB man sat upon a heap of broken reef near the
face, with his broad palms supporting his chin.
His thin, hollow cheeks showed between the out-
spread fingers, a sickly yellow in the candle light.
One candle in a spiked holder burned against the
side of the drive. Two billies and ttvo full crib-bags
hung near on dog-hooks driven in an upright leg,
and at the man’s feet lay a couple of picks
and a shovel. Kyley sat with his back to the
face, staring with glowing, vindictive eyes into
the gathered gloom down the drive, where the
passage to the shaft was choked to the roof with
splintered timber and fallen mullock, and where
the head of a second man was dimly visible. Only
the head and shoulders of this otner were free; the
rest of his body was hidden under the debris.
The second man was thrown face downwards;
across his back, pinning his arms, lay the great
cap-piece, which alone seemed heavy enough to
have crushed the life out ol him. Beyond this
the tumbled reef and splintered slabs were piled
to the roof.
But the buried miner was not dead. The tough
red-gum log forced down by the mighty pressure
had ploughed its way diagonally down the side of
the drive, and pinched him to the floor, stopping
when the pressure of another inch must have been
followed by certain and speedy death. A stout
iron truck was jammed under the log beside him,
torn and doubled like a card-board box. The
young man could lift his chin a few inches from
the floor of the drive, and turn his face from one
side to the other, but was incapable of any other
movement.
Presently he spoke. His voice came with an
effort, and sounded feebly shrill, like that of a
very old man.
“Dick, Dick ! in the name o’ God, speak man !
D’ye think there’s a chance for us ? ”
Dick Kyley dropped his hands, and there was
an expression of grim satisfaction in his gaunt
face as he replied deliberately, “There’s a chance
fer me, William,”
The buried man lifted his clay-smirched face,
startled by the other’s tone, and gazed eagerly at
his mate, and continued gazing for fully a minute,
puzzled and frightened by the incongruous levity
in the face that confronted him. Then, the posi-
tion becoming painful, he dropped his cheek in
the wet clay again.
“What d’ye mean?” he asked anxiously. “ Why
only fer you ?”
“ Because, William, I don’t think you’ve gob a
dog’s show.”
The reply was without a trace of sympathy ;
there was, in fact, a touch of malicious banter in
the mincing tone of the “William.” William
Hether had never been anything but “ Hether,”
or “ Bill ” to his shift mate before.
Again Hether looked anxiously into Kyley’s
face. Its cadaverous hollows were filled with
dark shadows, and the high-lights brought out the
salient features in a grotesque caricature that
struck Hether as simply fiendish. He turned
from the sight, with a new horror in his heart.
“This ain’t no time to fool a man, Dicx,” he
said humbly. “ How can there be any chance fer
you if I ain’t in it ?”
Kyley arose, plucked tne candle from tbe wall,
and advancing close to his mate held the flame
low down, and showed him a small pool of water
fathered upon the floor within 18 Inches of hU
ace.
“ That’s why,” he said.
Hether understood, and a ory broke from his
lips.
“ Keep it back, Dick 1” he gasped.
“William,” said Kyley, calmly replacing the
candle and resuming ids former position on the
reef, “you’re a fool. That water’s coming in
from the face, as usual. The fall has dammed the
gutters, and it can’t get away; consequently, in
less’n five hours the pool will be above your ears.
And you know what that means.”
“ But you can build a dam around me. Get the
shovel—quiok 1 Make a dam with that loose
reef, an’ the clay off the floor. Dick, Dick 1 give
us a chance, for God’s sake, man 1 ”
Hether stopped short, staring at the ether who
sat calmly regarding him. Presently he spoke
again in a quavering whisper :
“ You won’t see a man drown without lendin’ a
hand t’ help him? ”
“No, I won’t see it,” replied Kyley, “because
I’m goin’ to douse this light. A candle burns up
the air, an’ I’ll want all there is here, I reckon,
before the boys reach me.”
Driven almost wild with terror, a terror
occasioned no less by the grim significance of
Kyley’s leering countenance and the brutality of
the words than by the horrors of his position,
Hether began to plead piteously, with tears and
meanings. The pain of broken bones and the
sickness of exhaustion had quite unmanned “ Big
Bill Hether,” but his agony did not touch the
heart of Kyley, who seemed to have forgotten that
death also threatened him in the delight that the
young man’s sufferings awakened within his
breast.
“ Why’ve yeh rounded on me, Dick ? What’ve
I done—what’ve I ever.done? ” moaned the help-
less man.
“ I’m not goin’ to lift a finger to keep you out
of hell,” answered the other, “because of her,
William —because of Hannah.”
Bill turned his face to the light again, and once
more he stared at Kyley, sharply, inquiringly,
reading every line of his fateful countenance.
Then a groan of despair broke from him.
“ I’ll go away, Kyley,” he said —“true’s Christ,
if we get out I’ll go away, an’ you’ll never hear of
me again. Only make a dam. Quick, man,
quick, it’s coinin’! God I this is worse than
murder. Dick ”
The water having filled the depression at the
side of the drive was now running down and
forming a pool in the hollow under Hether’s chin.
Kyley turned and blew out the caudle. For a
long time Hether continued to supplicate in the
darkness, and Kyley, leaning comfortably against
the face, heard the thin voice, weakening to an
almost inarticulate whisper, beseeching by all
that is good on earth and holy in heaven for a
little grace—another poor chance of life, and
answered never a word. By a painful effort the
young man continued to keep his mouth above the
gathering water, but gradually the torture that
afflicted his extended neck became unendurable,
and now in his last extremity he railed at Kyley
as a murderer, and abused him with curses in
weak, childish tones that were nevertheless preg-
nant with passion, and sounded distinctly, and
with terrifying emphasis, in that black chamber
of death.
Suddenly there was silence. Dick Kyley
listened, and presently heard a bubbling sound in
the water. That ceased, and all was still. He
felt now that his vengeance was complete—that
Hether was dead, and at that moment the fierce
emotions of resentment and revenge-hunger that
had possessed and uphold him departed in a
breath, and left him weak and cowed. His limbs
trembled, and beads of perspiration gathered
about the roots of his hair and rolled coldly upon
his brow and cheeks. He was thinking, too, of
his own wretched case. He heard, fitfully, a
distant drumming, the sound of timber being
driven home, and knew that the rescue-parties
were working as hard as men may work; but
whether theirs would be a job of hours or days he
could not tell, and already he fancied he detected
some taint of vitiation in the air.
Dick Kyley, sitting alone in the blackness
of his prison, waiting for salvation death,
was soon the victim of an ungovernable fear,
a supernatural terror entirely new to him, and
the more awful for its novelty. From the
moment he believed Hether dead he began to fear
him. He strove with all the energy of his strong
sense to drive him from his thoughts, but do what
he might his mind would revert to the dread sub-
ject, and his eyes turn, staring intently into the
darkness where at times they seemed to detect a
yet blacker form in the pitch-black night that
filled the drive—the shape of the dead man’s head.
The horror grew, and with it an agonising convic-
tion that Hether’s dead face was staring at him
with dead but seeing eyes. Imagination had
pictured the pallid cheeks stained with blood and
clay, and the wide, accusing eyes, till the vision
became a reality to him. Tortured beyond endur-
ance, Kyley fumbled in his pocket, and found a
match, which he struck upon the shovel blade. As
the light filled the chamber a groan of relief
broke from the miner’s laboring breast. Only the
back of Hether’s head was visible ; his face was
sunk to the temple in the water. Dick extin-
guished the match —his last—and sat down again,
only to struggle with another relay of horrors that
presently arose against him.
William Hether still lived. He had discovered
that by taking a deep breath and sinking his face
till tile forehead rested upon the clay he was en-
abled to allay the pain in his neck and to con-
tinue the struggle. He persisted in this course,
noiselessly, for the sound of the rescuers at work
had filled him with a glorious hope, and with that
hope had come a fear that Kyley might be moved
to murder him if he thought his rescue possible.
So another hour fled. The water in the drive,
which had now found a broad level, continued to
rise slowly. Kyley had lost the power of appre-
ciating time, and sat huddled against the wall,
distraught with fear and despair. Hether’s face
was haunting him again, standing forth visibly,
threatening and awful in the tomb-like darkness.
His mad fancy stretched every hour of his im-
prisonment into a long day, and he believed that
it was his fate to be stifled by the foul gases from
his mate’s decomposing corpse. Even now the
taint was in his nostrils. Although he was listening
all the time with agonised intensity, he no longer
heard the hammering of the miners beyond; his
mind was too full of its unspeakable fear—he
awaited the attack of the inhuman thing that his
irresponsible faculties had fashioned out of the
impenetrable gloom at the end of his narrow
prison.
At this crisis, Hether called again in a piercing
voice full of the supreme terror
“ Help ! help ! Kyley, you murderer ! fiend !
devil ”
At the first sound of the voice, Kyley sprang
back against the end of the drive, and shrieked,
with all the power of his lungs, again and again;
and there he remained, crouched down, pressing
his face into the gravel, clutching his ears, shiver-
ing and moaning.
Three hours later the rescuers broke through,
and found Hether under the fall, with his head in
a pool of water, dead, and Kyley squatting at the
face, babbling of spectres and devils.
It is still Mr. Richard Kyley’s quaint belief
that ho is a conspicuous figure in hell.
EDWARD DYSON.

Live and let live I
Ed. Bulletin, — A statement of facts to
explain even Mr. Lonsdale’s question in N.S.W,
Assembly, the other night, re payment for over-
time to temporary and permanent railway hands
during the wool season. lam a temporary hand,
employed with others at Newcastle. When I
sought employment, I asked for two or three
months’ constant work during the season. I was
promised nothing, but, with the first wool, was
put on with others, being at once told, “You
will only be paid 6s. a day ” (nine hours). This
is how it pans out. On starting, I have to sign a
book ; but I mustn’t sign it till the foreman tells
me. I start work at 6 a.m., and work right on,
allowing for meals, if necessary, till 5, 6 or 8 p.m.,
or, as happened once, 8.30 p.m. (when I made
three hours and a half overtime), and come at six
next morning. For the same class of work, private
firms are paying Is. an hour, and Is. 6d. an hour
overtime. Then, by-and-by, 1 ask if we are paid
for overtime, and am told ‘‘No; you must take
it out.” “ And supposing I don’t happen to
have sufficient time in ? ” “ Then you are pro-
portionately short in your pay.” Next, I am
told to come at nine, but find there is nothing
doing, but—l “can look in again at two, and see
if anything turns up.” Nothing turns up; but I
“had better hang round,” and so on. _ As
a matter of fact, for the coming fortnight’s
pay I have, so far, but four days in out
of a possible nine—during all of which time I have
been hanging round. Then I have to wait another
week to get my money. Of course, that means
on the average a very small wage. To be con-
stantly on the job it is preferable to live in town,
but in town rents are high, so I ask for the usual
employes’ privilege—a season ticket at half-rates,
so that I may live in the cheaper suburb, but am
refused because I am only a temporary hand—not
casual, mind you. Had I the luck to be a casual,
I would consider myself equal to the rest of the
wool-jammers, and worth Is. an hour. Certainly
if they picked competent men in a casual way
they would have to pay the current rate of Is. an
hour. An instance ; We are told on Saturday,
“Look round on Monday.” We are there at
6 o’clock. After some hesitation we are started
and work the usual hours till about 4 o’clock.
At 4.25 we are sacked and permanent hands finish
the work. That gives us about ss. 4d. for the
day’s work, and for that day at least we are
debarred from picking up anything else. Our Bd.
an hour only amounts to the Government estab-
lished minimum wage when we are employed for
the full nine hours. Now, is this sweating ?
or is it “commercial principles”?—F., Newcas-
tle, 21/10/’95,

“A Traveller of Finance."
A BANKING INTERVIEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He pushed open the door and walked in with the
confidence of a bailiff about to take possession.
“Is the princi -pawl within?” he enquired, with
the air of a newly-created peer, addressing the
teller standing behind the counter of a bank that
had not reconstructed, and whose pride in conse-
quence about equalled that of the speaker’s
manner.
“Do you mean the manager ?” came the query
from the officer, experienced in all sorts and con-
ditions of callers.
“I wish to see the prinox-pawl of this establish-
ment 1”
“Do you particularly want to see him?” en-
quired the officer, suspiciously.
“I should not have called otherwise,” came the
reply in tones so sharp, and withal so sug-
gestive of capital in the background, that the
usually quick-discerning official was completely
disarmed, and banishing all doubt of the hona-Jidcs
of his visitor, he politely requested him to “step
around and go in at the door on the left.” Follow-
ing this precise direction the stranger marched
round the counter and knocked peremptorily at
the door upon which was a brass-plate with the
word “ Manager ” largely engraved thereon, so
that all who approached might read. Barely
waiting for the “Come in ” the visitor turned the
handle and entered, carefully closing the door
behind him.
“ You are the principal#?, I presume ? ” he said,
addressing a man busily engaged writing.
“lam the manager. Do you wish to speak
with me ? ”
“ I have called to see if you require a traveller
of finance ? ” spoken calmly.
“A what?” gasped the manager,
“ A. traveller of finance. A gentleman to repre-
sent your—firm —your great and wealthy corpora-
tion 1"
“ Ah, I see,” replied the manager, who had
recovered his presence of mind. “I am afraid,
however, yon have made a mistake in calling here,
You see,” with befitting dignity, “we don’t
require to do business on those lines. You will
sxcuse me just now. I am very busy," and the
aianager dipped his pen in the ink, instead of the
less considerate method of bluntly asking his
visitor to retire.
Then the latter burst into tears, wringing his
hands piteously, “ I have a sick child at home—
I should say a wife who is in bed with paralysis—
oh, for the love of God, give me help ! ” and the
man looked through his tears at the calm and
unemotional face of the bank representative. As
the lattet gave no sign of being touched, but only
began to write, the man stopped his tears, and,
blushing an indignant red, he exclaimed; “You
doubt my statement, sir ? ”
“I said nothing,” replied the manager, tlintily.
“But you looked it j you are a hard lot —you
men of bad overdrafts. Your vaults are bursting
with the savings of the poor—and men like me
have to walk the streets ” .
“I can’t have that soit of talk here. Come, you
must go out-” and the bank man rose up from his
Beat in just wrath, and spoke in threatening tones.
“ Will you give me threepence ? Your bank can
stand that, I suppose ? ”
“ My bank doesn’t stand impudence and loafers.
Come, go.” And the manager made as if to assist
the exit of his visitor.
“ You mean that ? ” said the man, speaking and
looking as if he thought he might forgive the
miserable bank official.
“Come, now, I want to get to work,” spoke the
latter again, angrily.
The tears were pumped up again.
“ Have you no feeling for my sick child ?”
“You spoke of your paralysed wife just now,"
corrected the official, cruelly.
“My mother is dead. Speak not of her,” said
the man, theatrically. “She never begot such a
son as you. I bid you good day, sir," The
speaker waved his hand magnanimously. “ Your
bank withstood the shock of ’93. The more shame
that you turn me away. I scorn your paupers’
gold. Good day, director!”
He had opened the door and was standing with
half his body through. He looked long and
silently, and with compassionate reproach in his
eye, which was directed to the bank-man’s bald
head bent low over the blotting-pad. The will-
force must have been weak, probably spent by the
nervous energy of the preceding dialogue, for the
manager did not raise his head. The man stood
in undecided attitude. He made a move to go,
then halted and looked again at the bald crown of
the “official head.”
“ Charity, thy name is— woman,” came the
words in Hamlet tones. Again the tears flowed,
and amid a snivelling the departing customer
cried out : “ What does our precious master say?
He says : ‘Bless they who curse ye 1’ ” With a
bang the door was closed, and the manager was
alone once more in his room. Passing out of the
bank-chamber the stranger looked across to the
young man behind the counter.
“ Banks are close corporations,” he said.
“ Adieu, young friend. Be kind to your
mother ! ”

Dear Bulletin, —The platypus has been
caught at Nebo, 68 miles S.VV. of Mackay, and
nearly three degrees north of Baffle Creek. —
Toby. Winton(Q.)
At Bundaberg (Q.) the police recently
examined Kanakas coming into town and relieved
them of sheath-knives, revolvers, cartridges and a
curious implement of native invention —viz., a
“grappler.” A “grappler ”is the iron portion of
a garden-rake fitted with straps to the hand—
points well-sharpened.
The wealthy Jews of London are be-
ginning to kick against the system which
“sweats” their charity funds fpr the enrichment
of the Moses who deals in “ slops.” Twenty
thousand hungry co-religionists (mostly Poles) are
employed in the clothing trade at an average
remuneration of 12s. a week, and the difference
between 12 “bob" andalivingwage has to bedrawn
from the charities, otherwise the 20,000 Chosen
would ascend to a region where shoddy garments
are unknown. The Jewish Chronicle reminds its
subscribers that this draining of their charities to
support sweated tailors takes the form of a sub-
vention of wages, or an annual bonus to the slop-
trade. Hence a proposal to organise the East End
Jews on communal lines, start co-operative
factories with a large lump sum of money down,
and bring Moses and his middlemen to confusion.
A Black Heathen .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Topknot was a nigger with a bad reputation. He
was a good enough station-boy when it so pleased
him, but when it did not please him there was
, , .
only one way of arguing the point with him, and
that was by means of a slip-rail. There was,
however, one man in the district who could
manage Topknot by moral suasion, and that man
was Ogilvie, of Bokkimarra, and he kept his
~ “ , J _ ,
method a secret. Whatever the influence he
exerted was, it was a potent one, and Topknot, a
powerful aborigine of more than Ogilvie’s stature
and physique, never disobeyed him, even when
mad-drunk. For all that the power was not one of
• . , i ,
affection, and it was rumoured that, night or day,
Ogilvie was never without his revolver, nor was
he Bver known to let Topknot ride or walk behind
bi m .
T , „,. , . . . ,
It was the standing mystery of the district, and
was solved in an extraordinary way. One day
lopknot came home with a bullet-wound, at least
~ f , ■, . . , ~,
it looked suspiciously like one, although he ex-
plained that he had staked himself, and as it was
just inside his thigh, it might possibly have hap-
pened that way. Ogilvie had been out on a
different, part of the run; when he returned he
dressed the wound and Topknot was laid up in
camp for some time. But young Halkett, who
was gaining colonial experience, said that on going
suddenly into the room he came unawares upon
Ogilvie extracting two empty cartridge-cases from
his pistol and putting two fresh cartridges in.
Topknot was soon about. A flesh-wound
quickly heals on a blackfellow, and he seemed
more under Ogilvies influence than ever. Soon
after that a horse fell on Ogilvie, and so bruised
his leg that he had to lie up. It was a dry time
and the fires were about. Halkett and all hands
went out to burn round a paddock, leaving the
old cook to look in and see after Ogilvie now and
again. The kitchen and men’s hut were no
distance from the cottage where Ogilvie and
Halkett lived, but the old man was deaf and
heard nothing, until, thinking it was about time
to see how the boss was getting along, he went in.
Guessing he might be asleep he slipped off his
shoes and crept in noiselessly, meaningjnsttopeep
into the room, but what he saw there gave him a
t urn -
Topknot was standing by Ogilvio’s bed. The
latter’s revolver lay on the ground and he himself
appeared to have something tied over his mouth
and round his arms. Topknot had a butcher’s
knife in his hand, and appeared about to carve
Ogilvie whose shirt was torn open at the breast,
Dan, the cook, was a tough and plucky old man.
He knew he had no chance against Topknot and
he could see the case was one for instant action—
there was no time to go back to the kitchen for a
gun. Drawing his sheath-knife, which was old,
sharp and worn to a point as a cook’s knife should
be, he sprang forward and stabbed the naked
black fair in the spine. Topknot dropped. The
cook cut the bandages round Ogilvie s arms and
Ogilvie motioned eagerly for his pistol, which Dan
picked up and gave to him.
Ogilvie emptied the last of the five chambers into
“That makes the third time he has attempted
my life,” said Ogilvie when explaining matters
“You see this scar on my breast’’—exposing a
cicatrice like an elongated V. 1 “It was made
lonig ago by a burn I got when trying to save a
woolshed on fire. One day in the wet season wo
were swimming some horses across the river. I
was stripped, of course, and I noticed Topknot
gtart and look stlange ly at me when ho saw it. I
suppose I stared back pretty hard, but over after
that ho would do anything I told him without a
murmur. I guessed what it was but could not
make full enquiries without giving myself away.
However, 1 found out by degrees that this scar
W as i den ti C al with a mark out on certain blacks
only, a very few of them being scattered amongst
many tribes. They are equally feared and hated,
as they are supposed to possess great power for
evi ]\ * for I had.not slept all night,
and but for Dan he would have had me on toast,
How x managed not to betray it by my eyes when
I saw Dan, I don’t know.”
“ Why did you let him off the other times)”
asked Halkett.

and th marked men . Do you know what he

From Westralia comes news of the death
of John Garner, who, years ago, was condemned
to death in Lincolnshire for the murder by poison
Q t his wife and mother-in-law. The sentence was
commuted to transportation for life to Westralia.
Garner always asserted his innocence, alleging
that a servant, with whom he had improper rela-
tions, had committed the double murder. A
petition with 20,000 signatures was, after a lapse
of years, presented to Homo Secretary Matthews,
asking for a free pardon. This was granted, but
Garner declined to accept it, unless Government
compensated him for the business he had lost at
the time of his conviction. This Government
declined to do, and Garner elected to remain
and die in Western Australia,

“THE BULLETIN.'
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—onstralla’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
*‘Tlie Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
li the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of Sta'
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Priso
System.
A United Australia and Protection agains
the World,
Australia for the Australians. —The chea
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and th
cheap European pauper to be absolute!;
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be
State monopoly.
The election of Ministers by Parlia
ment, instead of Party Government o
rather Government by Contradiction.
Anew Parliamentary System—one House t
be elected by constituencies as at present
the other to be chosen by the whol
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Lif
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers o
the day. It is only the remainder that is founc
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
arge fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
ami follow its_ lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the _ date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. I hey are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form-printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established A us-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Oause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than and
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the bright as t
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
